Navigating the Theory of Contracts: The Chapter Outline as a Roadmap
Detailed table of contents for the chapter on contract theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the five-group structure of al-Zarqa's chapter on contract theory and identify the logical sequence from formation to dissolution.

Core Idea
The chapter on contract theory is organized into five groups: introduction, formation, effects, dissolution, and classification.

This lesson introduces the table of contents for Chapter Six of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. Rather than diving into substantive rulings, we first map out the territory: twenty-one chapters arranged in five thematic groups. Understanding this map is essential before studying any single contract rule, because it shows how each topic relates to the whole.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five groups
Scan the outline and list the five groups (zumar) into which the chapters are classified. Note the chapter ranges for each group: introductory (27–28), formation (29–37), effects (38–44), dissolution (45), classification/overview (46–47).
The groups follow a logical progression: from foundational concepts to the lifecycle of a contract (creation, operation, termination) and finally a taxonomy.
2
Examine the introductory chapters
Look at Chapters 27 and 28. They provide important preliminary information and define five key technical terms. These are the building blocks for all subsequent chapters.
Mastering these terms is essential; they recur throughout the entire chapter.
3
Trace the formation sequence
Chapters 29 to 37 cover contract formation. Start with the essence of the contract (Chapter 29), then the formula (30), general conditions (31), contractual will (32), simulation (33), and defects of consent like duress (34), deception (35), mistake (36), and impediment of performance (37).
This sequence moves from structure to validity to vitiating factors.
4
Follow the effects and dissolution
Chapters 38 to 44 discuss the effects of a valid contract: effectiveness (38), bindingness (39), locusness (40), authority of will (41–42), conditions (43), and a comparative view (44). Then Chapter 45 covers dissolution (rescission and voidance).
Effects logically precede dissolution; a contract must first exist and produce effects before it can be dissolved.
5
Understand the final overview
Chapters 46 and 47 provide a survey of the most important named contracts and a classification system. This helps in applying general theory to specific contract types.
This section synthesizes the entire chapter by mapping general principles onto concrete examples.

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Islamic Jurisprudence. Overall Outline of Chapter Six: The theory of contracts is the most ramified and voluminous theory in this introduction, and it includes a large number of chapters that can be classified under five groups: First group: Introductory chapters (Chapters 27 and 28). Second group: Chapters related to the formation of the contract and related matters (Chapters 29-37). Third group: Chapters related to the effects of the contract and related matters (Chapters 38-44). Fourth group: A chapter related to the dissolution of the contract (Chapter 45). Fifth group: Includes an overview and classification of contracts (Chapters 46 and 47). Due to the ramification of the groups, we have provided below a diagram showing the relationships between the chapters. Understanding these relationships is a basic condition for a good understanding of contract theory. 1 - Introductory chapters: Detailed outline of Chapter Six. Chapter 27: Important introductory information. Chapter 28: Five important technical terms. 2 - Formation of the contract. Chapter 29: The essence of the contract. Chapter 30: The formula of the contract and related matters. Chapter 31: General conditions for the validity of contracts. Chapter 32: Contractual will (its types, hiddenness, absence). Chapter 33: Simulation in contracts (collusion and jest). Defects of consent (or impurities of will). Chapter 34: Duress. Chapter 35: Deception and pure error. Chapter 36: Mistake. Chapter 37: Impediment of performance. 3 - General effects of the contract and related matters. Chapter 38: Effectiveness. Chapter 39: Bindingness. Chapter 40: Locusness. Chapter 41: Authority of contractual will, general overview. Chapter 42: Islamic ijtihad on authority of contractual will. Chapter 43: Contractual conditions. Chapter 44: Authority of contractual will in foreign jurisprudence. - Dissolution of the contract. Chapter 45: Dissolution of the contract (rescission and voidance, etc.). E - Overview and classification of contracts. Chapter 46: General overview of the most important nominate contracts. Chapter 47: Classification of contracts. Page 378.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents the detailed table of contents for Chapter Six of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm, which is devoted to the theory of contracts in Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that this chapter is the most extensive in the book and organizes its 21 chapters (from Chapter 27 to Chapter 47) into five main groups: introductory material, formation of contracts, effects of contracts, dissolution, and a final overview with classification. He emphasizes that understanding the relationships between the chapters is essential for grasping contract theory. The outline itself is a roadmap for the reader, showing the progression from foundational concepts (like the essence and formula of a contract) to more advanced topics (such as defects of consent, simulation, duress, and mistake), followed by the legal effects (effectiveness, bindingness, etc.), then dissolution, and finally a survey of specific nominate contracts and their classification. This structure reflects a systematic approach typical of Hanafi juristic methodology in analyzing contractual obligations.

Key Terms
الزمرة (al-zumra): A group or cluster of chapters sharing a common theme. The outline divides the 21 chapters into five 'zumar'.
تكوين العقد (takwin al-‘aqd): The formation of a contract, covering its essential elements, conditions, and validity factors.
آثار العقد (athar al-‘aqd): The legal effects produced by a valid contract, such as bindingness and enforceability.
انحلال العقد (inhilal al-‘aqd): Dissolution of a contract, including both rescission (faskh) and automatic voidance (infisakh).
عقود مسماة (‘uqud musamma): Named or nominate contracts, such as sale, hire, gift, etc., that have specific rules in fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The chapter on contract theory is structured in five sequential groups: introduction, formation, effects, dissolution, and classification, providing a systematic framework for studying contractual obligations.

Classification of Contract Conditions: General vs Specific
Introduces classification of conditions for contract validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and specific conditions (shara'it 'amma and shara'it khassa) and identify which contracts require additional specific conditions.

Core Idea
Every contract must satisfy general conditions; some contracts also require specific conditions unique to their type.

In the previous chapters, we learned about the constituents of a contract: the parties, subject matter, and pillars. Now we turn to the conditions that must be attached to each constituent for the contract to be validly formed. This passage introduces a fundamental classification: conditions that apply to all contracts (general) and those that apply only to certain contracts (specific). Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti when analyzing contract validity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the constituents of a contract
The passage refers to the two parties, subject matter, and pillar. Ensure you know these from previous chapters.
2
Define general vs specific conditions
General conditions are required in every contract; specific conditions are additional requirements for particular contracts, e.g., witnesses for marriage.
3
Note examples of specific conditions
The passage gives: witnesses for nikah, delivery in real contracts, and prohibition of suspending commutative contracts. These illustrate how specific conditions arise from the nature of the contract.

Chapter Thirty-One: General Conditions for the Formation of Contracts. Distinguishing between General and Specific Conditions - The Seven General Conditions and Their Explanation - The Consequence of Missing Any Condition of Formation. The two contracting parties. 1/31 - We saw in the previous two chapters the constituents of the contract, which are: the two contracting parties, the subject matter, and the pillars; and we touched upon general discussions related to these constituents. For each of these constituents, there are legal conditions that must be fulfilled for the contract to be formed and concluded. These conditions of formation in contracts are of two types: - General conditions, which must be present in every contract. - Specific conditions, which are required in some contracts but not others. Some contracts have been singled out by the Lawgiver with additional conditions beyond the general conditions, such as the requirement of witnesses for the formation of marriage (nikah) unlike other contracts, and delivery in real contracts as previously mentioned, and the non-suspension of the contract on a condition in commutative contracts and transfers of ownership like sale and gift, for suspension invalidates them as we will see in the study of contractual conditions (see: 46/26-27). Those specific conditions for some contracts are mentioned in their respective chapters. As for the general conditions that are our subject here, they are seven conditions:
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of conditions for contract formation. The author recalls that a contract has constituents (the two parties, subject matter, pillar) and that each constituent has legal conditions. He then distinguishes two types: general conditions (shara'it 'amma) that apply to all contracts, and specific conditions (shara'it khassa) that apply only to certain contracts. He gives examples: witnesses for marriage, delivery in real contracts, and the prohibition of suspending a sale or gift on a condition. The passage notes that specific conditions are covered in their respective chapters, while the chapter will focus on the seven general conditions. The list of the seven conditions is not provided here; it begins on the next page.

Key Terms
شرائط عامة (shara'it 'amma): Conditions that must be present in every contract for its formation.
شرائط خاصة (shara'it khassa): Conditions required only for specific types of contracts, in addition to general conditions.
انعقاد (in'aqad): The legal formation of a contract, making it binding upon fulfillment of conditions.

Named and Unnamed Contracts in Hanafi Fiqh
Introduces division of contracts into named and unnamed categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between named and unnamed contracts in Hanafi fiqh and identify the significance of this classification for legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A named contract has specific shari'ah rulings; an unnamed contract is valid but lacks predefined rules.

Contracts in Islamic law are classified by whether the legislator has established specific names and rulings. This classification is foundational for muftis, as it determines whether detailed provisions apply or general principles are used. This lesson introduces the two categories and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of naming contracts
Recognize that contracts are named by people, scholars, or the legislator based on need and common usage.
The name alone does not make it a named contract; specific rulings are required.
2
Define named contracts (عقود مسماة)
These are contracts for which shari'ah has prescribed a specific name and a set of detailed rulings, such as sale (bay'), lease (ijarah), marriage (nikah), etc.
They are also called 'definite contracts' (عقود معينة).
3
Define unnamed contracts (عقود غير مسماة)
These are contracts that either lack a specific distinguishing name or have no special rulings legislated for them. They are governed by general contract principles (e.g., general permission of contracts).
Their validity is not denied, but they must meet general shari'ah conditions.
4
Recognize the practical difference
For named contracts, a mufti can apply established rules. For unnamed contracts, he must rely on general maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) and istihsan.
Improper classification leads to incorrect fatwa.
5
Note the counts and Hanafi basis
Fiqh books list 25 well-known named contracts; the Majalla includes 18. The presentation in the source is predominantly Hanafi.
This taxonomy is essential for systematic fatwa work.

Source Text

Chapter Forty-Six: Overview of Topics on the Most Important Named Contracts 1/46 - Named and Unnamed Contracts: Contracts are many types, some differ from others in names and rulings according to their subjects. And the contract, after need calls for it and it becomes common, people or the legislator or scholars assign to it a name that distinguishes it from others. Hence contracts are divided into two groups: - Named contracts, for which legislation has established names and specific rulings. They are also called definite contracts. - And unnamed contracts, which have not been given a specific distinguishing name, or for which legislation has not arranged specific rulings. 2/46 - As for named contracts, the well-known fundamental ones in fiqh books are twenty-five contracts. The Majalla of Justice contains eighteen of them. The Majalla differs in some of its order from fiqh books, just as fiqh books themselves differ in their order. (1) Our presentation of contracts here is largely based on the Hanafi school. (2) It is not sufficient for a contract to be considered named that it has a name if legislation has not established specific rulings for it.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the classification of contracts in Islamic law. The author distinguishes between named (musamma) and unnamed (ghayr musamma) contracts. Named contracts are those for which shari'ah has prescribed specific names and rulings, such as sale, lease, marriage, etc. Unnamed contracts lack such specific provisions, though they may still be valid under general principles. The author notes that the standard fiqh works list 25 main named contracts, while the Majalla al-Ahkam al-Adliyya (Ottoman code) includes 18. The passage emphasizes that having a name alone is insufficient; there must be legislated rules. The presentation follows the Hanafi school predominantly.

Key Terms
عقد مسماة (aql musamma): Contract with a specific name and legal rules set by legislation.
عقد غير مسماة (aql ghayr musamma): Contract without a specific name or without specific legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Forty-six
D) Thirty-two

Key Takeaway
Contracts in Islamic law are divided into named (musamma) with specific rulings and unnamed (ghayr musamma) governed by general principles – a distinction essential for Hanafi fiqh and fatwa.

Division (Qisma): Dual Nature of a Property Partition
Defines division and classifies its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qisma (division), distinguish between voluntary and judicial division, and identify the contractual nature of each according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Division of joint property involves both segregation and exchange; voluntary division is an explicit contract, judicial division carries an implied contractual effect.

Contracts are the backbone of property transactions. A particularly subtle concept in Hanafi fiqh is qisma—the partitioning of jointly owned assets. This passage unpacks the legal reality behind the physical act of dividing a shared property. It reveals that each partner receives not only his original share but also an exchanged portion from the other. Critically, the method of division—by mutual consent or by court order—determines whether it is an explicit contract or a mere judicial act with an implicit contractual element. Understanding this duality is essential for a mufti when advising on property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the legal definition of qisma
Read the definition: qisma is the division of joint property into distinct portions. Recognize that it is not a simple physical cut but a transfer of ownership rights.
The term 'qisma' comes from the root q-s-m, meaning to divide or share.
2
Identify the two components: ifraz and mubadala
In any division, each partner receives his original share via segregation (ifraz) and the remaining portion via exchange (mubadala) from the other partner. This double character gives the division its contractual flavor.
Ifraz separates what was already owned; mubadala is a transfer of ownership requiring mutual consent or its equivalent.
3
Distinguish voluntary vs. judicial division
Voluntary division (ridaiyya) is an explicit contract because it rests on the free agreement of all partners. Judicial division (qadaiyya) is ordered by a court; it is not an explicit contract but an authoritative removal of common ownership. However, it operates with an implied contractual effect (aqd dimni) out of legal necessity.
This distinction is crucial for fatwa: voluntary division follows general contract rules, while judicial division has its own regime.
4
Apply the maxim: 'al-aqd al-ṣarīh fī al-riḍāiyya wa al-ḍimnī fī al-qaḍāiyya'
Internalize the rule that only voluntary division is an explicit contract; judicial division is only a removal of shuyūʿ with a deemed contract. This affects issues like options (khiyārāt) and defects.
The Hanafi position is that judicial division validly transfers ownership perforce, so the implicit contract suffices for transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

فلو كان الشيء مشتركاً مناصفة مثلاً فقسم نصفين، يكون كل شريك إنما يملك في الواقع نصف النصف الذي عين له فقط، وباقيه لشريكه الآخر. فيعتبر كل منهما آخذاً في قسمه المعين ما يخصه من الأجزاء بطريق الإفراز، وآخذا للباقي بطريق المبادلة على ما يخصه في قسم شريكه. والقسمة تكون تارة رضائية باتفاق الشركاء؛ وتارة قضائية يجريها القضاء بطلب بعض الشركاء جبراً على باقيهم. فالقسمة التي تعد عقداً من العقود الصريحة، إنما هي الرضائية. أما القضائية فليست عقداً صريحاً، بل هي إزالة للشيوع بطريق القضاء، ولكن يعتبر فيها عقد ضمني مقدر اقتضاء في عمل السلطة القضائية، كما تقدم في نظرية الملكية (ر: ف ۷/۲۳).
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and What Pertains to Them in Jurisprudence. If a thing is jointly owned in equal shares, for example, and it is divided into two halves, each partner in reality only owns half of the half assigned to him, and the rest belongs to his other partner. So each is considered to take in his assigned portion what belongs to him from the parts by way of segregation (ifraz), and to take the rest by way of exchange (mubadala) for what belongs to him in his partner's portion. Division is sometimes voluntary by agreement of the partners, and sometimes judicial carried out by the court at the request of some partners coercively over the others. The division that is considered an explicit contract is only the voluntary one. As for the judicial, it is not an explicit contract, but rather a removal of common ownership through the judiciary, although an implicit contract is considered as required in the work of the judicial authority, as previously stated in the theory of ownership.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage opens the sixth chapter of the work, introducing the theory of contracts. It then discusses the concept of division (qisma) of jointly owned property. The author explains that when joint property is divided, each partner receives a portion that includes both their original share (by segregation) and a share from the other partner (by exchange). Division is classified into two types: voluntary (ridaiyya) and judicial (qadaiyya). Voluntary division is considered an explicit contract (aqd sarīh) because it is based on mutual agreement. Judicial division, on the other hand, is not an explicit contract; it is a court-ordered removal of common ownership. However, it implicitly contains a contractual element by legal necessity (iqtida). This aligns with the Hanafi view that judicial partition validly transfers ownership perforce. The passage references earlier discussion in the theory of ownership, and this distinction is important for understanding later contract classifications.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): Division of jointly owned property into distinct portions among the partners.
الإفراز (al-ifraz): Segregation of each partner's original share from the joint property.
المبادلة (al-mubadala): Exchange where each partner gives up some of his original share to the other to equalize portions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of division is considered an explicit contract?
A) Judicial division
B) Voluntary division
C) Both voluntary and judicial
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Voluntary division is an explicit contract; judicial division is a removal of common ownership with an implied contractual effect.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
The Structure of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm: A Map of Classical and Contemporary Fiqh
Index of chapter headings for both volumes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major thematic divisions of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm and locate chapters relevant to Hanafi contractual theory, sources, and capacity.

Core Idea
al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm maps the entire structure of Islamic jurisprudence from sources to modern codification.

This lesson introduces you to the table of contents of Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's monumental work. Understanding this map is essential for navigating the book's deep discussions on Hanafi principles, contract theory, and the historical development of fiqh. As a mufti, you will often need to consult this structure to find the relevant section for a given legal issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major parts (abwab) of the book
Scan the contents to see that the book is divided into eight sections (abwab), each covering a broad theme: Introduction, Sources, History, Codification, Property, Contracts, Sanctions, and Capacity.
These eight sections form the backbone of al-Madkhal.
2
Locate chapters on key Hanafi topics
Find the specific chapters for istihsan (Chapter 4), custom (Chapter 6), contract substance (Chapter 29), and capacity stages (Chapters 59-63). This shows where to look for detailed Hanafi legal theory.
Hanafi fiqh emphasizes istihsan and custom as subsidiary sources.
3
Connect chapters to modern fatwa contexts
Notice the appendices on riba (usury), zina punishment, and codification from fiqh. These indicate how al-Madkhal addresses contemporary issues relevant to fatwa.
A mufti dealing with modern financial contracts can turn to Chapters 27-47 (contracts) and Chapter 55 (causes of invalidity).

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents (fihris) for the entire work 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It lists the eight main sections (abwab) and their chapters (fusul) across two volumes. The author provides an overview of the structure of the book, covering introductory topics, sources of fiqh, historical development of fiqh, codification, property theory, contract theory, legal sanctions, and capacity/guardianship. This is front matter intended to give the reader a roadmap of the book's content. There are no specific fiqh rulings discussed here; rather, it is a reference guide to the subjects treated in the work. The text includes page numbers (649, 650) indicating the end of the index. The OCR is clean and reliable.

Key Terms
المدخل الفقهي العام (al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm): The title of the book by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, meaning 'The General Introduction to Fiqh', covering foundational concepts in Islamic jurisprudence.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A subsidiary source of fiqh where a mujtahid 'deems something better' and prefers it over apparent analogy.
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-‘Adliyyah): The Ottoman codification of Hanafi fiqh in civil matters, a landmark in legal reform.
التمليك والإباحة (al-tamleek wa al-ibahah): Two key concepts in the theory of property: tamleek is transfer of ownership (as in sale or gift), and ibahah is mere permissibility to use without ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which chapter discusses the difference between tamleek and ibahah?
A) Chapter 26
B) Chapter 23
C) Chapter 48
D) Chapter 59

Key Takeaway
The table of contents reveals that al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm systematically covers sources, history, property, contracts, sanctions, and capacity—critical for any mufti seeking comprehensive Hanafi fiqh principles.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Mapping the Second Half of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm
Table of contents for the second half

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six major sections of the second half of al-Madkhal and state the central theme of each.

Core Idea
A mufti's mastery begins with knowing where to find the law.

This lesson navigates the second half of Mustafa al-Zarqa's comprehensive introduction to Islamic fiqh. The table of contents reveals a systematic progression from property theory through universal legal principles—each section a pillar of juristic understanding. Familiarity with this structure equips the mufti to quickly locate relevant chapters when addressing contemporary issues.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the overall structure
The second half contains two parts: Part One (Sections 5-6) and Part Two (Sections 7-10). Each section is a major thematic block.
Note that the book is divided into two parts; this lesson covers the second half.
2
Identify Sections 5 and 6 on property and contracts
Section Five covers the theory of property (definition, causes, division, characteristics). Section Six delves into contracts: formation, will, defects, effects, and dissolution.
These are foundational for understanding ownership and transactions.
3
Identify Sections 7 and 8 on sanctions and capacity
Section Seven addresses legal sanctions (fixed and discretionary penalties, nullity, invalidity). Section Eight covers legal capacity (stages from womb to maturity) and guardianship.
Sanctions govern remedies; capacity determines who can act.
4
Identify Sections 9 and 10 on custom and universal principles
Section Nine analyzes the theory of custom (origin, types, authority, conflict with texts). Section Ten presents the universal legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah), including the Majalla principles.
Custom and maxims are critical tools for ijtihad and fatwa.

Commentary
This passage presents the table of contents for the second part of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It outlines the structure from the Fifth Section on Property Theory through the Tenth Section on Universal Legal Principles. The book systematically covers property, contracts, legal sanctions, legal capacity and guardianship, custom, and legal maxims. This comprehensive overview demonstrates the breadth of the work, which aims to provide a general introduction to Islamic fiqh with a modern analytical approach. The inclusion of appendices and indices indicates a scholarly apparatus for further study.

Key Terms
نظرية الملكية (nazariyyat al-milkiyyah): Theory of property in Islamic fiqh, covering definition, causes, division, and characteristics.
نظرية العقود (nazariyyat al-‘uqud): Theory of contracts in Islamic fiqh, including formation, will, defects, effects, and dissolution.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa‘id al-kulliyyah): Universal principles of Islamic fiqh, such as 'matters are judged by intentions' and 'certainty is not removed by doubt'.

Introduction and Front Matter

Navigating al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm: Structure and Key Topics
Book table of contents outlining structure and topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to navigate the table of contents of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm and identify the main sections relevant to Hanafi fiqh methodology, legal capacity, custom, and universal legal maxims.

Core Idea
A mufti must know the book's map to locate rulings and maxims quickly.

The table of contents of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa reveals a comprehensive framework for Islamic jurisprudence. It is divided into a historical introduction and then systematic treatments of legal capacity, custom, and universal rules. For a mufti, this outline is a navigational tool to find the relevant theoretical bases for fatwa-making.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main parts
Recognize that the book has a general introductory and historical part (Part I), followed by systematic chapters on legal capacity, custom, and universal rules.
Part I covers the meaning of fiqh, sources, and history.
2
Locate the section on legal capacity
Find the chapters under 'al-Bab al-Thamin' (Chapter 8) dealing with stages of ahliyyah, impediments, and guardianship.
Appendices provide additional critique and a new perspective.
3
Find the section on custom
Locate 'al-Bab al-Tasi'' (Chapter 9) on custom, covering its definition, types, authority, and conflicts with texts.
Custom is a secondary source heavily used by Hanafis.
4
Identify the section on universal rules
Find 'al-Bab al-'Ashir' (Chapter 10) on qawa'id kulliyya, including the Majalla rules and evidence rules.
These maxims aid in analogical reasoning and fatwa consistency.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed table of contents for the first volume of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It outlines the structure of the book, which is divided into two main parts: a general introductory and historical section, and then systematic treatises on legal capacity, custom, and universal rules. The first page covers later chapters on legal capacity (stages, impediments, guardianship), custom (definition, types, authority, conflict with texts), and universal fiqh rules (history, explanation of the Majalla rules, evidence rules). The second page provides a more detailed index for the first part, listing the introductory material, the meaning of fiqh, and the sources of Islamic law (primary and secondary). Notably, the secondary sources include istihsan, istislah (masalih mursala), and custom, with an appendix on siyasah shar'iyyah. This structure reflects al-Zarqa's comprehensive approach to usul al-fiqh and fiqh theory, emphasizing the integration of both classical and modern considerations.

Key Terms
عوارض الأهلية (‘awāriḍ al-ahliyya): Impediments to legal capacity; factors that affect a person's capacity to acquire rights and perform legal acts.
العرف (al-‘urf): Custom; repeated practices accepted by people that have legal weight as a secondary source of law.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawā‘id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that summarize many fiqh rulings, such as 'certainty is not removed by doubt'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Table of contents listing volume chapter titles.

This lesson introduces you to the table of contents of Volume Two of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam, a foundational work for understanding the framework of Islamic jurisprudence. While this passage contains no rulings, mastering the structure of such works is essential for efficient fatwa research and legal reference.

This volume covers general juristic principles (like contractual will) and specific contracts. It bridges theory and practice, preparing the reader for applied fiqh.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Introduction to the Theory of Legal Sanctions in Islamic Jurisprudence
Chapter heading and table of contents for theory of sanctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main categories of legal sanctions (disciplinary and civil) and list the specific types under each as outlined by Mustafa al-Zarqa.

Core Idea
Legal sanctions in Islam are divided into disciplinary (penal) and civil, each with distinct types and effects.

This lesson introduces Chapter 7 of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm, which systematically explores the theory of legal sanctions (al-mu'ayyidat al-shar'iyya) in Islamic jurisprudence. The outline reveals a clear taxonomy: sanctions are either disciplinary—including fixed penalties (hudud, qisas) and discretionary ones (ta'zir)—or civil, which involve nullity (butlan) or corruption (fasad). Understanding this classification is essential for any faqih or mufti when determining the legal consequences of an act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching subject
Read the chapter title and outline. The chapter is about 'The Theory of Legal Sanctions'—mechanisms that enforce Sharia rulings.
Sanctions are both worldly (dunyawi) and religious (ukhrawi), but here the focus is on worldly effects.
2
Distinguish the two main branches
The outline splits sanctions into two broad categories: disciplinary (ta'dibiyya) and civil (madaniyya). Disciplinary sanctions are penal; civil sanctions affect the validity of contracts and transactions.
This mirrors the Hanafi distinction between 'uqubat and ahkam.
3
Locate the subcategories of disciplinary sanctions
Under disciplinary, there are fixed penalties (hudud and qisas) covered in Section 49, and discretionary penalties (ta'zir) in Section 50.
Fixed penalties are divinely prescribed; ta'zir is left to the judge's discretion.
4
Locate the subcategories of civil sanctions
Civil sanctions are divided into nullity (butlan) and corruption (fasad). Sections 51-53 cover butlan; 54-57 cover fasad.
In Hanafi fiqh, butlan makes an act void ab initio, while fasad makes it irregular but potentially curable.
5
Note the comparative dimension
Section 58 compares the theory of nullity with foreign jurisprudence, indicating a comparative legal analysis.
This reflects the author's broader methodology.

Chapter Seven
The Theory of Legal Sanctions in Islamic Jurisprudence

Overall Outline for Chapter Seven

Section 48: Origin of Sanctions, Their Definition, and Classification.
Disciplinary Sanctions: Penalties:
Section 49: Fixed Penalties (Hudud and Qisas).
Section 50: Discretionary Penalties or Ta'zir.
Civil Sanctions: Nullity (Batlan)
Section 51: Theory of Nullity - General Overview.
Section 52: Cause of Nullity.
Section 53: Consequences of Nullity.
Civil Sanctions: Corruption (Fasad)
Section 54: Theory of Corruption - General Overview.
Section 55: General Causes of Corruption.
Section 56: Consequence of Corruption, etc.
Section 57: Position of Law Regarding Corruption, and Our View on the Subject.
Section 58: Theory of Nullity in Foreign Jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This is the opening of Chapter 7 of "al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm" by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. The chapter is titled "The Theory of Legal Sanctions in Islamic Jurisprudence" (نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية). The page provides an outline of the chapter's contents, which are divided into sections (fusul) numbered 48 to 58. The classification shows that the author first discusses the origin, definition, and classification of sanctions. Then he divides sanctions into two main categories: disciplinary/criminal (ta'dibiyya) which include fixed penalties (hudud and qisas) and discretionary penalties (ta'zir); and civil sanctions which include nullity (butlan) and corruption (fasad). The chapter concludes with a comparative look at the theory of nullity in foreign jurisprudence. This outline sets the stage for a detailed study of how Islamic law enforces its rulings through various types of sanctions, a topic central to the understanding of legal enforcement (iftā') and the broader philosophy of Islamic law.

Key Terms
المؤيدات الشرعية (al-mu'ayyidāt al-shar'iyyah): Legal sanctions in Islamic law; mechanisms that enforce legal rulings and obligations.
الحدود (al-ḥudūd): Fixed, divinely prescribed punishments for specific crimes (e.g., theft, zina).
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retributive penalty for homicide or bodily harm, equivalent harm for harm.
التعزير (al-taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses not covered by hudud or qisas.
البطلان (al-butlān): Absolute nullity; an act that is void from its inception, producing no legal effects.
الفساد (al-fasād): Irregularity; an act that is defective but may be corrected or has limited effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the outline of Chapter 7, which two topics are covered under 'Civil Sanctions'?
A) Hudud and Qisas
B) Nullity (Butlan) and Corruption (Fasad)
C) Ta'zir and Diya
D) Penalties and Fines

Key Takeaway
Legal sanctions in Islamic jurisprudence are systematically classified into disciplinary (penal) and civil categories, with fixed and discretionary penalties under the former, and nullity and corruption under the latter.

Legal capacity in Hanafi fiqh is analysed through four branches: definition, stages, impediments, and guardianship.

In this brief lesson, we will look at the organisational skeleton of the Hanafi theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and guardianship (wilayah) as presented by Mustafa al-Zarqa. Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship
Overall Plan of the Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship
1/59 – We have divided the chapters on the theory of legal capacity and guardianship into four branches:
First branch: Containing Chapter 59 on the basis of legal capacity, its definition, and its types.
Second branch: Comprising five chapters (60)-(64) on the five stages of a person's legal capacity according to the phases of his life: the stage of [conception?], then childhood, then discernment, then puberty, then maturity.
Third branch: Containing Chapter 65 on impediments to legal capacity.
Fourth branch: Containing Chapter 66 on guardianship or legal representation. Then we conclude Chapter Eight with two important appendices:
The first of them explains the criticisms of the traditional presentation of impediments to legal capacity.
The second clarifies the advantage of our scientific focus on the theory of legal capacity in a way that solved its problems.
Source pages p. 734

Commentary
This passage is the introduction and outline (mukhattat ijmaili) of Chapter Eight of the book, which deals with the theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and guardianship (wilayah). The author breaks the chapter into four main branches. The first covers foundational issues: the basis of legal capacity, its definition, and types. The second branch addresses five life stages affecting capacity: the stage of being a fetus (ijtinān – referring to the unborn), childhood (tufūlah), discernment (tamyīz), puberty (bulūgh), and maturity (rushd). The third branch discusses temporary impediments (awarid) that affect capacity. The fourth branch examines guardianship (wilayah) and legal representation (niyabah shar‘iyyah). Finally, two important appendices are mentioned: one critiquing traditional presentations of impediments, and another highlighting the author's scientific approach that solved problems in this area. This is purely structural and serves as a roadmap for the reader.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity: the fitness of a person to acquire rights and duties and to exercise them.
ولاية (wilāyah): Guardianship: the authority to act on behalf of another, especially minors or incapacitated persons.

Introduction to Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
Chapter title announcing the beginning of the theory of custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of custom (al-urf) and explain its status as a subsidiary source in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Custom is a subsidiary source in Hanafi law, used where no primary text exists, provided it is consistent with Shariah.

This chapter heading marks the beginning of an exploration of custom (al-urf) in Islamic jurisprudence. The Hanafi school gives significant weight to consistent and reasonable customs, often preferring them over analogical reasoning. In this lesson, we will set the stage for understanding the theory of custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the topic
This chapter is about the theory of custom (al-urf), a recognized subsidiary source of law in Hanafi fiqh.
Custom is what people commonly practice in their dealings, as long as it does not contradict Shariah.
2
Recall the definition
Al-urf is defined as commonly accepted and practiced customs that are consistent with the principles of Shariah.
The Hanafis consider urf a valid source for deriving rulings in transactional matters.
3
Assess its authority
Custom is a subsidiary source, used only when there is no explicit text (nass) from Quran or Sunnah.
In Hanafi usul, urf can override qiyas in certain cases, especially in muamalat.
4
Anticipate contents
The chapter will likely cover the definition of urf, its proofs, conditions for validity, and practical applications.
Expect discussions on how to distinguish valid custom from corrupt custom.

Introduction to the Theory of Custom
Introducing the Theory of Custom and outlining its four branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of custom as a subsidiary source of Islamic law and outline the four branches of the theory of custom as presented by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa.

Core Idea
Custom is a dynamic subsidiary source; rulings change as custom changes, ensuring fiqh remains relevant.

Earlier in his work, al-Zarqa briefly introduced custom (al-urf) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law. Now, in the opening of Chapter Nine, he announces a full treatment of the theory of custom. This passage sets the stage by highlighting the enormous number of rulings affected by custom and the principle that rulings evolve with changing custom—a key manifestation of Sharia's adaptability. He then maps out four branches that will structure the entire discussion, guiding our study of custom's definition, authority, conflicts with primary evidences, and relation to temporal change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Claim
Read the passage to find the author's main assertion about custom's importance. He says it is a 'major theory', 'occupies a great space', and that rulings based on it 'cannot be counted' and 'never end'. Note the language of magnitude and constant renewal.
This claim sets the tone for why custom matters in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the Dynamic Principle
The passage states that rulings subject to custom 'change with its change' and are 'in constant renewal'. This is a fundamental principle: custom is not static; it evolves, and fiqh rulings adapt accordingly. Reflect on why this demonstrates the capacity for eternity in Sharia.
This principle distinguishes custom from primary sources like Quran and Sunnah, which are fixed.
3
Outline the Four Branches
The author divides the discussion into four branches. List them: (1) General overview, definition, classification; (2) Authority and conditions for considering custom; (3) Cases of custom contradicting legal evidence; (4) Comparison between custom and customary evidences, and change of time. Note the sections where each will be covered.
This outline will guide your subsequent study of the theory.
4
Connect to Previous and Future Content
The passage references an earlier discussion on sources of fiqh where custom was introduced. It also positions the theory of custom as the 'last of the fundamental juristic theories'. Consider how this theory fits within the broader framework of al-Zarqa's work.
This contextualization helps you see the structure of the entire book.

Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom.

1/67 - Preliminary and overall outline of the theory of custom:
Earlier in the discussion of sources of Islamic jurisprudence, we gave a brief defining word about custom considering it a subsidiary source of rulings, and we referred the details to the theory of custom, which resides here as the last of the fundamental juristic theories.
Now we treat below this important major theory that occupies a great space in Islamic jurisprudence, and upon which are based numerous rulings from various chapters and juristic principles, whose number cannot be counted and whose renewal never ends, because the rulings that Islamic law subjects to custom change with its change; they are in constant renewal, and this is one of the greatest manifestations of the capacity for eternity in the structures of the Sharia and its jurisprudence.
2/67 - The discussion on the juristic theory of custom is divided into the following four branches:
First branch: (Sections 67-68) Contains a general overview, definition, and classification.
Second branch: (Sections 69-70) Concerns the authority of custom and the conditions for its consideration.
Third branch: (Sections 71-74) Therein we discuss cases of custom contradicting legal evidence.
Fourth branch: (Sections 75-78) Therein we compare between custom and customary evidences, then discuss issues of custom and change of time.
Source pages p. 809

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to Chapter Nine on the Theory of Custom. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, reminds us that custom was previously introduced as a subsidiary source of law. Now he provides a detailed exposition. Custom is a major theory because it underpins countless rulings across all chapters of fiqh, and these rulings evolve as custom changes, demonstrating the adaptability and timelessness of Islamic law. He then outlines four branches that will be covered in subsequent sections: (1) general overview, definition, and classification; (2) the authority and conditions for considering custom; (3) cases where custom conflicts with primary evidences; (4) a comparison between custom and customary evidences and the effect of changing times. This structured map prepares the reader for the detailed discussion that follows.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom – a subsidiary source of Islamic law that is considered in rulings when not contradicting primary evidences.

Prefatory Outline of the Theory of Custom (al-Urf)
Prefatory outline of the theory of custom in Islamic jurisprudence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the structure of the Hanafi theory of custom (al-urf) and identify the four branches of its discussion.

Core Idea
Custom is a subsidiary source of rulings that changes with time, giving Islamic law flexibility and timelessness.

In this prefatory outline, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa introduces the theory of custom ('urf) as the last of the fundamental juristic theories in Islamic law. He emphasizes that custom is a subsidiary source of rulings, and the rulings it governs are dynamic—they change as custom changes, which demonstrates the capacity of Islamic law for eternal relevance. The discussion is structured into four branches: definition and classification, authority and conditions, conflict with textual evidence, and comparison with customary evidence with respect to changing times.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of custom in Islamic jurisprudence
Recognize that custom ('urf) is a subsidiary (tabi) source of rulings, not primary. It operates within the framework of Sharia and is used to derive rulings where no clear text exists or to interpret texts.
Custom is not independent but works alongside primary sources like Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas.
2
Grasp the significance of custom's dynamism
Note that rulings based on custom change as custom changes. This adaptability is a key feature of Islamic law that allows it to remain applicable across different times and places.
For a mufti, this means fatwas may change over time if the underlying custom changes.
3
Memorize the four branches of the theory
The author divides the discussion into four branches: (1) definition and classification, (2) authority and conditions for acceptance, (3) conflict with textual evidence, and (4) comparison with other customary evidence and change over time.
This structure provides a comprehensive framework for studying 'urf.
4
Identify the practical impact of each branch
Each branch addresses a different aspect: the first clarifies what counts as 'urf; the second when it is valid; the third how to resolve conflicts with texts; the fourth how to weigh it against other evidences and temporal changes.
A mufti must navigate these branches when applying custom in fatwa.

This passage is a prefatory outline from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It introduces the theory of custom ('urf) as a major juristic theory, positioning it as the last of the fundamental theories. The author explains that custom is a subsidiary source of rulings, and the rulings based on it are subject to change as custom changes, which demonstrates the flexibility and perpetual relevance of Islamic law. This outline sets the stage for the detailed analysis that follows.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom: recurrent practices accepted by people that serve as a subsidiary source of fiqh rulings.
مصدر تبعي (masdar tabi): Subsidiary source: one that is secondary to primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) and used to derive rulings when primary sources are silent or to interpret ambiguous texts.
سلطان العرف (sultan al-urf): Authority of custom: the extent to which custom can influence rulings, subject to conditions.

Al-Zarqa's Five Major Theories and the Deferred Sixth
Roadmap to the five theories and transition to general maxims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five major fiqh theories according to al-Zarqa and describe the author's plan to add a sixth theory on guarantees.

Core Idea
Al-Zarqa's five major theories cover all fiqh rulings; a sixth on guarantees was planned but deferred.

This passage is a roadmap from al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm.' It summarizes the five overarching theories that govern all fiqh rulings—property, contracts, legal effects, capacities/guardianship, and custom. The author also reveals his intention to include a sixth theory on guarantees, which he had to postpone due to space constraints. Finally, he signals the transition to the third and final section on general legal maxims.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the passage as a roadmap
Understand that this passage serves as a bridge between the previous section (on the development of ijtihadi rulings) and the upcoming section on general maxims. It recaps the conceptual framework underlying the entire work.
2
List the five major theories
From the text, extract the five theories: theory of property (الملكية), theory of contracts (العقود), theory of legal effects (المؤيدات), theory of capacities (الأهليات) and guardianship (الولاية), and theory of custom (العرف).
3
Identify the deferred sixth theory
Note the author's mention of a planned sixth theory on guarantees (نظرية الضمانات), which he had to omit from this volume. Recognize that he later published a partial treatment in 'Theory of Harmful Act'.
4
Understand the transition to qawaid kulliya
The passage ends by introducing the third section of the book, which is dedicated to general maxims (القواعد الكلية). This section systematizes the principles extracted from the earlier theories.

الفصل (۷۸) تغير الأحكام الاجتهادية لتطور الوسائل
لنظرية الملكية وقواعدها. وإذا كان فيه التزام عقدي خضع لنظرية العقود. وإذا كان فيه صحة أو فساد أو بطلان أو توقف خضع لنظرية المؤيدات ونظرية الأهليات وإذا كان في حكمه تأثير للعرف خضع لنظرية العرف. وإذا كان فيه تصرُّف بفعل أو قول أو فيه إثبات حق أو التزام خضع أيضاً لنظرية الأهلية والولاية والعقد.
·
فهذه النظريات الأساسية الكبرى تضرب نطاقاً حول أبواب الفقه وفصوله كلها يحيط بها ويهيمن عليها ويوجه أحكامها، وتمسك كل جهة منه بطرف من هذه الأحكام.
أن

القسم الثالث
مِنَ المدخل الفقهي العَامِ
القواعد الكلية
Chapter (78): Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to the Development of Means. ... It is subject to the theory of property and its rules. If it involves a contractual obligation, it is subject to the theory of contracts. If it involves validity, invalidity, nullity, or suspension, it is subject to the theory of legal effects (mu'ayyidat) and the theory of capacities (ahliyat). If custom influences its ruling, it is subject to the theory of custom. If it involves an act or statement or the establishment of a right or obligation, it is also subject to the theory of capacity, guardianship, and contract. So these major fundamental theories cast a sphere around all chapters and sections of fiqh, encompassing them, dominating them, and directing their rulings, and each aspect of fiqh is held by a part of these rulings. ... 78/5 - A sixth important theory remained in my research plan that I would treat and complete the set of major fundamental theories. That is the theory of guarantees (damanat), in which the juristic principles regarding the guarantee of properties, rights, and obligations in the dhimmah, the legal causes of liability (tadmin), types of guarantee (stable and transferable), etc. But I left the treatment of this theory now due to the narrowness of my time and the scope of this part; the topics of this part have expanded beyond the limit I had intended. Now we move to the third section of this work, which is the last section dedicated to presenting the general maxims in Islamic fiqh. (1) However, Allah facilitated that I published before this new edition of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm the 'Theory of Harmful Act' and published it as an independent book (Dar al-Qalam, Damascus, 1409 AH / 1988 CE), which covers a large part of the theory of guarantees that have other causes beyond the harmful act. Perhaps I will republish the 'Theory of Harmful Act' within the next volume (third) of this juristic series.
Source pages p. 897

Commentary
This passage is a transitional section from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It first summarizes the five major juristic theories (property, contracts, legal effects, capacity/guardianship, and custom) that govern all areas of fiqh. The author notes that each ruling falls under one of these theories. He then mentions a planned sixth theory on guarantees (damanat) which he had to omit from this work due to time and space constraints. However, he previously published a related work, 'Theory of Harmful Act,' covering a significant aspect of guarantees. Finally, he announces the transition to the third and final section of the book, which is dedicated to presenting the general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya) in Islamic fiqh. This serves as a roadmap for the reader, showing the scope of the work and the author's scholarly plan.

Key Terms
النظريات الأساسية الكبرى (al-nazariyyāt al-asāsiyya al-kubrā): The five major theories that encompass all fiqh rulings: property, contracts, legal effects (mu'ayyidat), capacities (ahliyat), and custom.
نظرية الضمانات (nazariyyat al-ḍamānāt): The proposed sixth theory covering juristic principles of guarantee, liability, and causes of compensation.

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Introduction to Major and Local Legal Maxims
Introducing the book's structure on maxims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between major (kulliyya) and local (mahalliyya) legal maxims in Hanafi fiqh and state their respective scopes.

Core Idea
Major maxims govern entire fiqh chapters; local maxims govern specific topics within them.

This lesson introduces the structural division of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm into its third section on major maxims and its tenth chapter on local maxims. While this passage contains no rulings, it provides a critical mental map: universal principles versus topic-specific rules. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for a mufti who must know when to apply a broad maxim versus a narrow one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and note that it distinguishes between 'al-qawa'id al-kulliyya' (major maxims) and 'al-qawa'id al-mahalliyya' (local maxims). These are the two levels of maxims discussed in the book.
The terms are given in Arabic; memorize their meanings.
2
Understand scope differences
Major maxims apply across many different chapters of fiqh (e.g., contracts, damages, worship). Local maxims are restricted to a single topic or chapter (e.g., rules specific to sales).
Think of major maxims as overarching principles, local maxims as specific tools.
3
Relate to book structure
The third section of the book is devoted to major maxims, and within it, the tenth chapter covers local maxims. This tells you the organizational logic: broadest first, then narrower.
Knowing the structure helps you quickly locate the relevant maxim when researching a case.
4
Recognize their function in iftaa
A mufti uses major maxims first to frame a case, then refines the ruling using local maxims that are specific to the type of transaction or act.
This two-step approach ensures consistency and precision in fatwa.

القسم الثالث من المدخل الفقهي العام القواعد الكلية الباب العاشر القواعد المحلية في الفقه الإسلامي
The Third Section of the General Jurisprudential Introduction: The Major Maxims. Chapter Ten: The Local Maxims in Islamic Jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage marks the structural transition of the book. It announces the beginning of the third major section (القسم الثالث) of 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, which is dedicated to the study of 'al-Qawa'id al-Kulliyya' (the major legal maxims). These are the overarching principles that apply across multiple chapters of fiqh. Following that, the tenth chapter (الباب العاشر) within this section focuses on 'al-Qawa'id al-Mahalliyya' (local maxims), which are more specific to particular areas of fiqh. The author is organizing the material so that readers first grasp the broadest maxims, then move to more particular ones. No rulings are stated here; it is purely a descriptive roadmap.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawā‘id al-kulliyya): The major legal maxims that are universal in scope, applying to many different chapters of Islamic jurisprudence.
القواعد المحلية (al-qawā‘id al-maḥalliyya): Local legal maxims that are specific to particular topics or chapters of fiqh, as opposed to universal maxims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) الباب العاشر
C) القسم الثالث
D) الباب التاسع

Key Takeaway
Major maxims (kulliyya) are universal and govern multiple fiqh chapters; local maxims (mahalliyya) are specific to particular topics.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Defining the General Legal Maxim (al-Qāʿida al-Kulliyya)
Definition and characteristics of the term qāʿida

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term qāʿida (legal maxim) according to the Hanafi usul al-iftaa tradition, distinguish it from a dābiṭ (specific control), and identify its essential characteristics.

Core Idea
A true qāʿida is a constitutional fiqh text expressed in general formulas; it governs multiple fiqh chapters.

The passage we are studying begins by introducing the concept of al-qawāʿid al-kulliyya al-fiqhiyya, general legal maxims that serve as constitutional principles in Islamic law. The author provides a precise definition that distinguishes true maxims from more specific rules. Understanding this definition is crucial for any mufti who wishes to apply maxims correctly in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Locate the author's definition of qāʿida in the passage. Note that it is described as a 'constitutional fiqh text' that expresses a general ruling in a few words using general formulas.
The definition appears in the discussion of the Majalla and al-Hamzawi's work.
2
Break down the components
Analyze the definition: 'constitutional fiqh text' (nass fiqhi dusturi), 'expresses a general ruling' (hukm kulli), 'in a few words' (bi ḍiʿḍi alfāẓ), 'using general formulas' (min ṣiyagh al-ʿumūm).
Each component is essential for a statement to qualify as a qāʿida.
3
Distinguish from a dābiṭ
The author contrasts a qāʿida with a ḍābiṭ juzʾī (partial control). A ḍābiṭ is specific to a particular fiqh topic, while a qāʿida applies across multiple topics.
This distinction is crucial for proper fatwa derivation.
4
Identify the legal effect
A qāʿida can be applied across many chapters of fiqh to derive derivative rulings. This cross-chapter applicability is the key test.
For example, 'al-umur bi-maqāṣidihā' applies in ibadat, muamalat, and other areas.
5
Reflect on the importance for fatwa
A mufti must correctly identify whether a statement is a qāʿida or a ḍābiṭ to apply it properly. Misclassification leads to incorrect fatwa.
The author's definition provides a clear criterion.

Chapter Ten: General Legal Maxims
7/80 - Then came the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, carrying in its forefront a large collection of these rules, selected from the most important of what Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi compiled, plus some other rules added to it. They reached ninety-nine rules in 99 articles (from article 2 to article 100), with which the provisions of the Majalla began after the first article that included the definition of fiqh and the division of its topics.
8/80 - After the Majalla, the scholar Sayyid Shaykh Mahmud al-Hamzawi, Mufti of Damascus during the reign of Sultan Abd al-Hamid, undertook an exhaustive survey of rules, controls, and principles in most chapters of fiqh beyond the rules of the Majalla. He compiled them in a book he named al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya fi al-Qawa'id wa al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya, clarified them with some examples, and arranged them according to the chapters of fiqh. It was the latest and most comprehensive work compiled under the name of legal rules and principles as far as we know, and he interspersed among the rules basic rulings he called 'fawa'id'. It was printed in Damascus in 1298 AH.
But the general rules in the sense we have explained, like those that came in the Majalla, are very few in it, and most of what is presented under the headings of qawa'id are only partial controls or basic rulings in specific topics from the chapters of fiqh.
And the author has another treatise specifically on rules of waqf rulings.
9/80 - This is a summary of the movement of codifying legal rules, then their compilation in the Hanafi school. And it appears from tracing the movement of authorship on rules that the Shafi'i jurists, then the Hanbalis, then the Malikis followed the Hanafis in that, then it moved to Shi'a scholars, in this historical order!
The eighth century Hijri was the most abundant in these compositions, as can be known from tracing what has been recorded in the catalogs of famous libraries also. And it is noted here that many works known as 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir', such as the book of Taj al-Subki and the book of Jalal al-Suyuti (Shafi'is), contain many of these rules, to the extent that Ibn Nujaym al-Hanafi mentioned in the introduction to his 'Ashbah' that he intended to compose it following the model of al-Subki's book.
However, the most famous works that are known and have reached us printed, from the three non-Hanafi schools, that discuss rules or bear the name of rules, are three important works:
1 - The book 'Qawa'id al-Ahkam fi Masalih al-Anam' by the Shafi'i jurist Imam Izz al-Din Abd al-Aziz ibn Abd al-Salam, died 660 AH.
2 - And the book 'al-Furuq' by the Maliki jurist Shihab al-Din Ahmad ibn Idris, known as al-Qarafi, died 684 AH, student of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam al-Shafi'i.
3 - And the book 'al-Qawa'id' by the Hanbali jurist Abd al-Rahman ibn Rajab, died 795 AH.
However, these books, despite their great value, do not contain the rules in the sense we have defined for the word 'qāʿida', i.e. the constitutional fiqh texts that express general rulings in a few words in general formulas, rather they contain basic divisions and controls on major fiqh topics:
a - The book of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam al-Shafi'i is based on objective fiqh chapters; he places the fiqh topic as a heading at the top of the chapter, then divides the rulings related to it and elaborates in detail with much explanation of the wisdom of legislation. It is more like a valuable fiqh introduction.
b - The book 'al-Furuq' by al-Qarafi clarified in its introduction that he wrote it to explain the differences between rules, and that he collected in it 548 rules, and explained each with appropriate branches, and the book came printed in four large volumes.
Source pages p. 909

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the codification of general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliya al-fiqhiyya) in the Hanafi school and beyond. The author traces the lineage: the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya (the Ottoman Civil Code) compiled 99 rules drawing from Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi. After the Majalla, Shaykh Mahmud al-Hamzawi produced al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya, which collected many rule-like statements, but the author notes that most of these are specific sub-rules (dawabit juz'iyya) rather than universal maxims. The key definition here is that a true qa'ida is a 'constitutional fiqh text' expressing a general ruling in a few words. The passage then shows how other schools followed the Hanafi lead, with the Shafi'is through al-Subki and al-Suyuti, then Hanbalis, then Malikis, and later Shi'as. The works of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, and Ibn Rajab are discussed, but the author argues that they do not meet his strict definition of qa'ida; instead they contain divisions and basic rulings on major topics. For a Hanafi mufti, understanding this history is important because it clarifies the difference between a universal maxim (kulli) and a specific fiqh rule, which affects how fatwas are derived and justified.

Key Terms
قاعدة (qāʿida): A constitutional fiqh text that expresses a general ruling in a few words using general formulas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a true qāʿida kulliyya?
A) It is a general ruling in a few words using general formulas.
B) It is a detailed exposition of a fiqh topic.
C) It is a collection of fatwas.
D) It is a branch of usul al-fiqh.

Key Takeaway
A true qāʿida kulliyya is a brief, general formula that applies across multiple fiqh chapters, distinct from a specific ḍābiṭ.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Tradition of Codifying Legal Maxims
Historical development of Hanafi maxim codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the historical development of compiling qawāʿid in the Hanafi school, from Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi to the Majalla and al-Hamzawi.

Core Idea
The Majalla’s 99 rules represent the peak of Hanafi maxim codification, drawing on earlier compilations.

The passage then describes how the Hanafi school led the way in collecting legal maxims. Beginning with the works of Ibn Nujaym (author of al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir) and al-Khadimi, the Ottoman Majalla codified 99 rules. Later, al-Hamzawi expanded the collection but included many specific rules. This lesson examines the key compilations and their significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier Hanafi compilers
The passage mentions Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi as the sources from which the Majalla selected its rules. Ibn Nujaym is the author of al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, a foundational work on maxims.
Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi are both Hanafi scholars.
2
Note the Majalla's role
The Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, the Ottoman civil code, compiled 99 rules (articles 2-100) drawing from Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi, plus additional rules. This was a landmark codification.
The Majalla was applied in Ottoman courts.
3
Analyze al-Hamzawi's work
After the Majalla, al-Hamzawi wrote al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya, which collected many rule-like statements. However, the author notes that most rules in it are partial controls (ḍawābiṭ), not true qawāʿid.
Al-Hamzawi's work is more comprehensive but less precise in classification.
4
Understand the author's critique
The author argues that only a few statements in al-Hamzawi's work meet the definition of a true qāʿida kulliyya. This highlights the difficulty of collecting universal maxims.
The critique helps refine the concept.
5
Summarize the Hanafi timeline
The Hanafi school led the codification, beginning with Ibn Nujaym, then al-Khadimi, then the Majalla, and later al-Hamzawi. This timeline shows the development of the genre.
Other schools followed later.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية الفقه
٧/٨٠ - ثم جاءت مجلة الأحكام العدلية تحمل في صدرها مجموعة كبيرة من هذه القواعد، مختارة من أهم ما جمعه ابن نجيم والخادمي، مضافاً إليه بعض قواعد أخرى. فبلغت تسعاً وتسعين قاعدة في ٩٩ مادة (من المادة / ۲ حتى المادة / ١٠٠ استهلت بها أحكام المجلة بعد المادة الأولى التي تضمنت تعريف الفقه وتقسيم مباحثه
۸/۸۰ - وبعد المجلة قام العلامة السيد الشيخ محمود الحمزاوي مفتي دمشق في عهد السلطان عبد الحميد باستقصاء القواعد والضوابط والأصول في معظم الأبواب الفقهية مما وراء قواعد المجلة. وقد جمعها في كتاب أسماه الفرائد البهية في القواعد والفوائد الفقهية وأوضحها ببعض الأمثلة ورتَّبها على أبواب الفقه فكان آخر وأوسع ما جمع باسم القواعد والأصول الفقهية فيما نعلم وهو ينثر بين القواعد أحكاماً أساسية يسميها فوائد» وهو مطبوع بدمشق سنة ۱۲۹۸ هـ .
•
ولكن القواعد الكلية بمعناها الذي بيناه كالتي جاءت بها المجلة قليلة جداً فيه وجل ما جاء فيه تحت عناوين القواعد إنما هو ضوابط جزئية أو أحكام أساسية في موضوعات خاصة من أبواب الفقه.
·
وللمؤلف رسالة أخرى في قواعد أحكام الوقف خاصة.
۹/۸۰ - هذا مجمل حركة تقعيد القواعد الفقهية، ثم جمعها
المذهب الحنفي. ويظهر من تتبع حركة التأليف في القواعد أن فقهاء الشافعية، ثم الحنابلة، ثم المالكية قد تابعوا الحنفية في ذلك، ثم انتقلت إلى علماء الشيعة، بهذا الترتيب التاريخي! .
وقد كان القرن الثامن الهجري أحفل القرون بهذه التأليف، كما يعلم
من تتبع ما سجلته فهارس المكتبات الشهيرة من المؤلفات أيضاً. ويلاحظ هنا أن كثيراً من المؤلفات التي عُرفت باسم «الأشباه
والنظائر ككتاب التاج السبكي وكتاب الجلال السيوطي الشافعيين، تشتمل على كثير من هذه القواعد حتى إنَّ ابنَ نُجيم الحنفي ذكر في مقدمة «أشباهه» أنه أراد وضعه على غرار كتاب السبكي الشافعي .
على أنَّ أشهر ما عرف ووصل إلينا مطبوعاً من مؤلفات المذاهب الثلاثة غير الحنفي، مما يبحث في القواعد أو يحمل اسم القواعد، ثلاثة مؤلفات هامة :
١ - كتاب قواعد الأحكام في مصالح الأنام للإمام الفقيه الشافعي
عز الدين عبد العزيز بن عبد السلام المتوفى سنة ٦٦٠ هـ .
۲ - وكتاب «الفروق» للفقيه المالكي شهاب الدين أحمد بن إدريس الشهير بالقرافي المتوفى سنة ٦٨٤هـ تلميذ العز بن عبد السلام الشافعي . - وكتاب «القواعد» للفقيه الحنبلي عبد الرحمن بن رجب المتوفى
سنة ٧٩٥هـ.
غير أن هذه الكتب على عظيم قيمتها لا تتضمن القواعد بالمعنى الذي حددناه لكلمة «القاعدة»، وهي النصوص الفقهية الدستورية التي تعبر عن أحكام كليَّة في بضعة ألفاظ من صيغ العموم، وإنما تتضمن تقسيمات وضوابط أساسية في موضوعات فقهية كبرى :
أ - فكتاب العز بن عبد السلام الشافعي مبني على فصول فقهية موضوعية، يَضَعُ فيها الموضوع الفقهي عنواناً في رأس الفصل، ثم يقسم المتعلقة به ويفصلها تفصيلاً فيه كثير من بيان حكمة التشريع. فهو
الأحكام
أشبه بمدخل فقهي قيم.
ب - وكتاب الفروق للقرافي قد أوضح في مقدمته أنه قد وضعه لبيان الفروق بين القواعد)، وأنه قد جمع فيه / ٥٤٨/ قاعدة، وأوضح كلاً منها بما يناسبها من الفروع وقد جاء الكتاب مطبوعاً في أربعة أجزاء كبار .
Chapter Ten: General Legal Maxims
7/80 - Then came the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, carrying in its forefront a large collection of these rules, selected from the most important of what Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi compiled, plus some other rules added to it. They reached ninety-nine rules in 99 articles (from article 2 to article 100), with which the provisions of the Majalla began after the first article that included the definition of fiqh and the division of its topics.
8/80 - After the Majalla, the scholar Sayyid Shaykh Mahmud al-Hamzawi, Mufti of Damascus during the reign of Sultan Abd al-Hamid, undertook an exhaustive survey of rules, controls, and principles in most chapters of fiqh beyond the rules of the Majalla. He compiled them in a book he named al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya fi al-Qawa'id wa al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya, clarified them with some examples, and arranged them according to the chapters of fiqh. It was the latest and most comprehensive work compiled under the name of legal rules and principles as far as we know, and he interspersed among the rules basic rulings he called 'fawa'id'. It was printed in Damascus in 1298 AH.
But the general rules in the sense we have explained, like those that came in the Majalla, are very few in it, and most of what is presented under the headings of qawa'id are only partial controls or basic rulings in specific topics from the chapters of fiqh.
And the author has another treatise specifically on rules of waqf rulings.
9/80 - This is a summary of the movement of codifying legal rules, then their compilation in the Hanafi school. And it appears from tracing the movement of authorship on rules that the Shafi'i jurists, then the Hanbalis, then the Malikis followed the Hanafis in that, then it moved to Shi'a scholars, in this historical order!
The eighth century Hijri was the most abundant in these compositions, as can be known from tracing what has been recorded in the catalogs of famous libraries also. And it is noted here that many works known as 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir', such as the book of Taj al-Subki and the book of Jalal al-Suyuti (Shafi'is), contain many of these rules, to the extent that Ibn Nujaym al-Hanafi mentioned in the introduction to his 'Ashbah' that he intended to compose it following the model of al-Subki's book.
However, the most famous works that are known and have reached us printed, from the three non-Hanafi schools, that discuss rules or bear the name of rules, are three important works:
1 - The book 'Qawa'id al-Ahkam fi Masalih al-Anam' by the Shafi'i jurist Imam Izz al-Din Abd al-Aziz ibn Abd al-Salam, died 660 AH.
2 - And the book 'al-Furuq' by the Maliki jurist Shihab al-Din Ahmad ibn Idris, known as al-Qarafi, died 684 AH, student of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam al-Shafi'i.
3 - And the book 'al-Qawa'id' by the Hanbali jurist Abd al-Rahman ibn Rajab, died 795 AH.
However, these books, despite their great value, do not contain the rules in the sense we have defined for the word 'qāʿida', i.e. the constitutional fiqh texts that express general rulings in a few words in general formulas, rather they contain basic divisions and controls on major fiqh topics:
a - The book of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam al-Shafi'i is based on objective fiqh chapters; he places the fiqh topic as a heading at the top of the chapter, then divides the rulings related to it and elaborates in detail with much explanation of the wisdom of legislation. It is more like a valuable fiqh introduction.
b - The book 'al-Furuq' by al-Qarafi clarified in its introduction that he wrote it to explain the differences between rules, and that he collected in it 548 rules, and explained each with appropriate branches, and the book came printed in four large volumes.
Source pages p. 909

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the codification of general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliya al-fiqhiyya) in the Hanafi school and beyond. The author traces the lineage: the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya (the Ottoman Civil Code) compiled 99 rules drawing from Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi. After the Majalla, Shaykh Mahmud al-Hamzawi produced al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya, which collected many rule-like statements, but the author notes that most of these are specific sub-rules (dawabit juz'iyya) rather than universal maxims. The key definition here is that a true qa'ida is a 'constitutional fiqh text' expressing a general ruling in a few words. The passage then shows how other schools followed the Hanafi lead, with the Shafi'is through al-Subki and al-Suyuti, then Hanbalis, then Malikis, and later Shi'as. The works of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, and Ibn Rajab are discussed, but the author argues that they do not meet his strict definition of qa'ida; instead they contain divisions and basic rulings on major topics. For a Hanafi mufti, understanding this history is important because it clarifies the difference between a universal maxim (kulli) and a specific fiqh rule, which affects how fatwas are derived and justified.

Key Terms
قاعدة (qāʿida): A constitutional fiqh text that expresses a general ruling in a few words using general formulas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the total number of rules included in the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya?
A) 99
B) 100
C) 99 following the first article
D) 548

Key Takeaway
The Majalla's 99 rules, drawn from Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi, represent the authoritative Hanafi codification of legal maxims.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Spread of Legal Maxims to Other Schools and a Critical Survey
Critical survey of non-Hanafi works on legal maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three major non-Hanafi works on legal maxims and explain why they do not contain true qawāʿid according to the Hanafi definition.

Core Idea
Works by al-Izz, al-Qarafi, and Ibn Rajab, despite their titles, are not collections of universal maxims but thematic fiqh expositions.

The passage concludes by surveying how other schools followed the Hanafi lead, but notes that the most famous works—Qawa'id al-Ahkam by al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Furuq by al-Qarafi, and al-Qawa'id by Ibn Rajab—do not actually contain maxims in the strict Hanafi sense. This lesson explores those works and the author's critique.

Guided Path
1
Note the historical order of adoption
The passage states that the Shafi'i jurists followed the Hanafis first, then Hanbalis, then Malikis, and later Shi'a scholars. This shows the Hanafi pioneering role.
The eighth century Hijri saw the most activity.
2
Identify the three key works
The three works are: Qawa'id al-Ahkam by al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam (Shafi'i), al-Furuq by al-Qarafi (Maliki), and al-Qawa'id by Ibn Rajab (Hanbali). All are titled 'qawa'id' or 'furuq' (differences).
These are the most printed and known works from non-Hanafi schools.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author argues that these books do not contain true qawāʿid (brief general formulas) but rather divisions and controls on major fiqh topics. They are more like systematic expositions.
The critique is based on the strict definition of qāʿida.
4
Examine each work's structure
Al-Izz's book is like a fiqh introduction, organized by chapters. Al-Qarafi's focuses on differences between rules. Ibn Rajab's is similar to the others. None use the terse general formulas of the Hanafi tradition.
The passage explains each briefly.
5
Evaluate the significance for a mufti
A Hanafi mufti using these works must understand they are not sources of qawāʿid but rather thematic discussions. They can still be valuable for understanding rulings and principles.
This prevents misuse in fatwa.

Chapter Ten: General Legal Maxims
7/80 - Then came the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, carrying in its forefront a large collection of these rules, selected from the most important of what Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi compiled, plus some other rules added to it. This passage is a historical overview of the codification of general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliya al-fiqhiyya) in the Hanafi school and beyond.
