Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
Historical Overview of Codification of Legal Maxims
Author critiques three major non-Hanafi works on qawa'id

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the extent to which the works of Ibn 'Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, and Ibn Rajab meet the Hanafi definition of a universal legal maxim (qawida kulliyya).

Core Idea
True qawa'id are concise universal principles; earlier works contain thematic discussions, not concise maxims.

This lesson examines a key historical passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa, who critiques three celebrated works on legal maxims from the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools. He argues that although these works are valuable, they do not contain what he defines as 'qawa'id'—concise, universal legal principles. Instead, they offer thematic discussions, basic rulings, and detailed elaborations. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone studying legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and the Hanafi approach to qawa'id.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's definition of a qawida
Extract from the passage the precise meaning of 'qawida' as a concise, universal, constitutional fiqh text. Understand that it must be expressed in a few general words and apply across multiple chapters.
Al-Zarqa uses the phrase 'constitutional fiqh texts' (نصوص فقهية دستورية) to emphasize their foundational nature.
2
Examine each of the three works against that definition
For each work (Ibn 'Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, Ibn Rajab), note how the author describes their content and why they do not fit his definition. Look for key phrases: 'thematic chapters', 'basic rulings', 'detailed explanations'.
Pay attention to the author's verdict: these works are valuable but not collections of qawa'id.
3
Analyze what each work actually contains
Summarize the actual content of each work: Ibn 'Abd al-Salam's is an introduction to fiqh topics; al-Qarafi's focuses on distinguishing rules in pairs; Ibn Rajab's treats each 'rule' as a broad topic for detailed discussion.
Al-Qarafi's work has scattered universal maxims but is not dedicated to them.
4
Reflect on the significance of this critique for Hanafi usul
Consider why al-Zarqa makes this distinction. It establishes the need for a separate, dedicated collection of universal maxims, which the Hanafi school later produced (e.g., Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir).
This critique helps avoid confusion between chapter-specific rules (dawabit) and universal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya).
5
Evaluate the value of these works despite their non-conformity
Acknowledge that the author calls these works 'highly valuable' and 'unprecedented'. Their value lies in their depth and contribution to fiqh, but they serve a different purpose.
A mufti should use them for gaining contextual understanding, not as direct sources of universal maxims.

على أنَّ أشهر ما عرف ووصل إلينا مطبوعاً من مؤلفات المذاهب الثلاثة غير الحنفي، مما يبحث في القواعد أو يحمل اسم القواعد، ثلاثة مؤلفات هامة :

غير أن هذه الكتب على عظيم قيمتها لا تتضمن القواعد بالمعنى الذي حددناه لكلمة «القاعدة»، وهي النصوص الفقهية الدستورية التي تعبر عن أحكام كليَّة في بضعة ألفاظ من صيغ العموم، وإنما تتضمن تقسيمات وضوابط أساسية في موضوعات فقهية كبرى :
أ - فكتاب العز بن عبد السلام الشافعي مبني على فصول فقهية موضوعية، يَضَعُ فيها الموضوع الفقهي عنواناً في رأس الفصل، ثم يقسم المتعلقة به ويفصلها تفصيلاً فيه كثير من بيان حكمة التشريع. فهو أشبه بمدخل فقهي قيم.
ب - وكتاب الفروق للقرافي قد أوضح في مقدمته أنه قد وضعه لبيان الفروق بين القواعد)، وأنه قد جمع فيه / ٥٤٨/ قاعدة، وأوضح كلاً منها بما يناسبها من الفروع وقد جاء الكتاب مطبوعاً في أربعة أجزاء كبار .
غير أنَّ هذا الكتاب أيضاً على عظيم قيمته وما تضمن من مباحث لم يسبق إليها، لم يجمع قواعد بالمعنى الذي نعنيه هنا، وإنما يريد من القواعد معنى الأحكام الأساسية في الموضوعات الفقهية الكبرى فهو يعرض هذه الأحكام الأساسية في كل موضوعين متشابهين؛ ويجلو الفروق بينهما فيها فيقول مثلاً:

Chapter 80: A historical glimpse at the codification of universal maxims and parallels. Like the book of Taj al-Subki and the book of Jalal al-Suyuti (Shafi'is), they contain many of these maxims, to the extent that Ibn Nujaym al-Hanafi mentioned in the introduction to his 'Asbahs' that he intended to base it on the model of al-Subki's Shafi'i book. However, the most famous of what has been known and printed from the works of the three non-Hanafi schools that discuss maxims or carry the name 'maxims' are three important works: 1. The book 'Qawa'id al-Ahkam fi Masalih al-Anam' by the Shafi'i jurist 'Izz al-Din 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn 'Abd al-Salam (d. 660 AH). 2. The book 'al-Furuq' by the Maliki jurist Shihab al-Din Ahmad ibn Idris known as al-Qarafi (d. 684 AH), student of al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam al-Shafi'i. 3. The book 'al-Qawa'id' by the Hanbali jurist 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Rajab (d. 795 AH). However, these books, despite their great value, do not contain maxims in the sense we have defined for the word 'maxim,' i.e., constitutional fiqh texts that express universal rulings in a few words of general formulations. Rather, they contain fundamental divisions and regulations on major fiqh topics: A) The book of al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam al-Shafi'i is based on thematic fiqh chapters. He places the fiqh topic as a heading at the top of the chapter, then divides the related matters and details them in great detail with much explanation of the wisdom of legislation. It is more like a valuable fiqh introduction. B) Al-Qarafi's 'al-Furuq' explained in its introduction that he wrote it to explain the differences between the rules, and that he collected 548 rules and explained each with appropriate subsidiary cases. The book has been printed in four large volumes. However, this book also, despite its great value and what it contains of unprecedented discussions, did not collect maxims in the sense we mean here. Rather, he intends by 'rules' the meaning of basic rulings in major fiqh topics. He presents these basic rulings in each two similar topics and clarifies the differences between them in them, saying, for example: the difference between the rule of 'insha' and 'khabar', and the difference between the rule where ignorance and gharar are effective and the rule where they are not among transactions, and the difference between the rule of granting ownership of usufruct and granting the right to benefit, and the difference between the rule of verbal custom and actual custom, etc. So he intends by 'rule' the meaning of the basic rulings on a particular topic, not the terminological meaning we have explained for it. However, in the book al-Furuq, many constitutional fiqh rules are scattered in its chapters on occasions of explaining rulings and establishing controls. C) The book of Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali based his discussions on one hundred and sixty rules and concluded with twenty-one benefits. It is likewise. He places under the title 'rule' a fiqh topic, then treats it with detailed explanation and admirable detail. It is a book of great value, carrying a wealth of fiqh that defies description. The author of Kashf al-Zunun described it as 'one of the wonders.' But in any case, it is not a collection of fiqh rules with general constitutional texts in the aforementioned sense.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological critique of three famous works on legal maxims from the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, sets the stage for his own work by clarifying what he means by 'qawa'id' (legal maxims): concise, universal, constitutional principles. He argues that the earlier works, while valuable, do not fit this definition. Ibn 'Abd al-Salam's work is more an introduction to fiqh topics; al-Qarafi's 'al-Furuq' focuses on distinguishing between rules in pairs, not on universal maxims; and Ibn Rajab's work treats each 'qawida' as a broad topic for detailed discussion. This is important for students to understand that the Hanafi tradition later systematized qawa'id as distinct from these earlier works. The passage thus establishes the need for a dedicated collection of universal legal maxims, which al-Zarqa aims to provide.

Key Terms
قاعدة (qā'ida): A constitutional fiqh text expressing a universal principle in a few general words.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawā'id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that apply across multiple fiqh chapters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do the three non-Hanafi works on qawa'id not meet his definition of legal maxims?
A) They are too short and lack detail.
B) They contain thematic discussions and basic rulings rather than concise universal principles.
C) They only cover worship matters.
D) They were written by non-Hanafis.

Key Takeaway
The author distinguishes true universal maxims from thematic discussions, emphasizing that precision in defining qawa'id is essential for sound legal methodology.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Author's Classification of General Legal Maxims
Author's methodology for classifying maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the author's classification of the general legal maxims into basic and branch categories and explain the purpose of this arrangement.

Core Idea
Forty basic maxims and fifty-nine sub-maxims form the backbone of Hanafi legal maxims.

This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains how the author chose to present the general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya) that were embedded in the Ottoman Majalla. Instead of a lengthy commentary, he provides a new classification: forty basic maxims with fifty-nine sub-maxims under them, arranged by subject and cross-referenced to the Majalla. This lesson guides you through his methodology so you can navigate the maxims effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall structure
Note that the author distinguishes two layers: basic maxims (40) and branch maxims (59). Understand that basic maxims are the primary rules, while branch maxims are derived from them.
The total is 99 maxims, but only 40 are considered foundational.
2
Examine the classification criteria
The author arranges the basic maxims by subject matter, placing those on guardianship and public administration first, then evidence at the end. Sub-maxims are listed under their relevant basic maxim.
This subject-based grouping aids systematic study.
3
Understand the numbering and formatting conventions
Basic maxims are numbered in words (e.g., 'forty') and printed in bold. Sub-maxims are indicated by alphabetic letters in regular type. This visual distinction helps the reader quickly identify the hierarchy.
The sequential count resets for each basic maxim.
4
Recognize the cross-referencing system
After each maxim, the author provides the corresponding article number from the Majalla. This allows easy lookup in the original code and its commentaries.
At the end of the chapter, an alphabetical index of maxims with page and article references is provided.
5
Appreciate the pedagogical purpose
The author states that studying these maxims gives 'immediate juristic aptitude' and illuminates Islamic jurisprudence. The classification is designed for educational efficiency, avoiding lengthy commentary while preserving practical application.
The method balances conciseness with depth, suitable for Sharia and law college curricula.

The Tenth Chapter: General Legal Maxims in Fiqh

2/81 - This, and since these general maxims were incorporated into the text of the Majalla and made part of it, all commentators of the Majalla have undertaken to explain them with commentaries varying in length and brevity, clarifying the juristic rulings that branch from them and what is excepted from them; they have also been treated in commentaries specific to them apart from the rest of the Majalla. So studying and clarifying these general maxims gives the student immediate juristic aptitude and casts revealing light on the horizons of Islamic jurisprudence and its principles, enabling the student to proceed in understanding with firm, steady steps and perceptive insight.
3/81/1 - It was part of my objective in this Introduction to make this third and last section an extensive commentary on these maxims with which the Majalla begins, but when I reached this last section I noticed that this Introduction had become lengthy in the first two sections, and that these maxims are sufficiently explained in most of the available commentaries on the Majalla, even if their style is not the educational method we have committed to following. 1
(1) The most extensive and esteemed commentary on these maxims of the Majalla, in my view, is the commentary of my father, Sheikh Ahmad ibn Sheikh Muhammad al-Zarqa (may Allah have mercy on them both). He explained these maxims on the occasion of teaching them to us at the al-Madrasa al-Thanawiyya al-Shar'iyya in Aleppo, then continued to expand their explanation and add to it everything he found related to any of these maxims from branches of rulings, restrictions, and exceptions in his continuous juristic readings and teachings over twenty years, so it became the most abundant of commentaries in benefits and evidence, and the most comprehensive of scattered points, such that its student grasps the outlines of fiqh. Death hastened him before its printing, so it remained in manuscript with me. Then Allah facilitated its printing a few years ago under the title '(Commentary on the Juristic Maxims)', then we reprinted a second edition in Dar al-Qalam, Damascus, 1409 AH/1989 CE.
978
Chapter (81): Commentary on the Maxims of the Majalla and Their Classification
Therefore, I turned away from expanding on the commentary of these maxims, and I decided to suffice now with presenting their texts classified and arranged in a new order, along with clarification of some of them with brief interpretation, and mentioning some examples from the branches of each maxim that indicate its juristic application, preserving the limits of the educational objective in the colleges of Sharia and Law.
I have distinguished in this classification the basic maxims from the other maxims that branch from them, so the basic maxims came to forty and the branch maxims to fifty-nine.
I arranged the forty basic maxims according to their subjects. I placed the maxims related to guardianship and public administration rights, then those related to evidence, at the end of all.
I made the sequential count of the maxims up to the last return to the forty basic ones, and I wrote their numerical rank in words not numerals, and in bold type to distinguish them.
As for the branch maxims derived from them, I distributed them under those basic maxims, so I placed under each basic maxim the maxims branching from it in regular type, and I symbolized the enumeration of those branch maxims with the series of alphabetic letters.
I committed to indicate after each maxim its article number in the Majalla due to the difference in arrangement, to facilitate reference to it in the Majalla and its commentaries. And for ease of revision, we will list at the end of the explanation of these maxims their bare texts arranged alphabetically by their first words, with an indication beside each of the chapter and paragraph number where the maxim and its explanation occur in this book, and its article number in the Majalla.
Source pages p. 913

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to the tenth chapter on general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya). He explains that these maxims were embedded in the Ottoman Majalla and have been commented upon extensively. He originally intended to provide a full commentary in this book but found that existing commentaries are sufficient, so he instead presents the maxims in a new classified arrangement: forty basic maxims with fifty-nine sub-maxims under them. He describes his organizational method: basic maxims numbered in words and bold, sub-maxims in regular type with alphabetic enumeration. He also provides cross-references to the Majalla articles for easy lookup. This sets the stage for the rest of the chapter, which will list and explain the maxims.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya): General legal maxims that encompass many fiqh rulings under a single principle; here referring to the set of ninety-nine maxims (40 basic, 59 sub-maxims) extracted from the Majalla.
القواعد الأساسية (al-qawa'id al-asasiyya): Basic maxims, of which the author identifies forty, that are the foundational principles from which other maxims branch.
المتفرعات (al-mutafarri'at): Branch maxims, fifty-nine in number, derived from the basic maxims and listed under them.
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman Civil Code (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya), a compilation of Hanafi fiqh rules including 99 general maxims.

Key Takeaway
The author classifies general maxims into 40 basic and 59 branch maxims, arranged by subject and cross-referenced to the Majalla, to facilitate systematic study.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The First Major Maxim: Matters Are by Their Intentions (الأمور بمقاصدها)
Introduces the first major maxim and its contract-focused subsidiary rule with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' to classify actions and contracts based on intention, and identify the subsidiary rule 'al-ibra fi al-uqud lil-maqasid' in practice.

Core Idea
Intention determines legal rulings: the same outward act can be a gift, loan, sale, or pledge based on your inner motive.

This lesson unpacks one of the five major maxims of Islamic jurisprudence: 'Matters are by their intentions.' It is a cornerstone of Hanafi reasoning, emphasizing that the inner motive (niyyah) overrides external form. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, also presents a subsidiary rule focusing on contracts: 'Consideration in contracts is for intentions and meanings, not for words and structures.' Through examples from murder, loans, lost property, and contracts like bay' al-wafa' and gift-with-compensation, we will explore how this maxim operates across fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the main maxim and its subsidiary rule
First, read the Arabic texts 'الأمور بمقاصدها' and 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني'. Understand that the first is the general principle and the second applies it specifically to contracts.
The subsidiary rule is derived from the main maxim and is considered a form of dabt (restrictive sub-maxim).
2
Apply the maxim to non-contractual actions: murder, money, lost item
Work through each example: (a) killing with intent vs. mistake gives qisas vs. diyah; (b) giving dirhams with intent to donate is a gift, with intent to lend is a loan; (c) picking up a lost item with intent to keep is usurpation (liable), with intent to return is trust (not liable). Note how the same outward act changes ruling based on intention.
This illustrates the maxim's broad scope beyond contracts.
3
Apply the subsidiary rule to contract examples: bay' al-wafa' and stipulation gift
For bay' al-wafa', the parties intend a security arrangement, so it takes the rulings of rahn (pledge) despite the form of sale. For a gift with stipulated consideration, the true intention is sale, so the contract takes all sale rulings (e.g., return for defect, recovery of price if ownership contested).
These examples show that the legal name (lafz) is secondary to the real economic meaning (ma'na).
4
Recognize the overarching Hanafi principle: intention governs over form
Conclude that the Hanafi school systematically prioritizes underlying intent over literal wording. This is a key difference from schools that may give more weight to the external expression.
This maxim is applied throughout ibadat, mu'amalat, and jinayat.

Chapter (81): Explanation of the Legal Maxims of the Majalla and Their Classification. Therefore I turned my attention away from expanding on the explanation of these maxims, and I saw fit to suffice now with presenting their texts classified and arranged in a new order, with clarification of some of them by brief interpretation, and mentioning some examples from the branches of each maxim indicating its jurisprudential application, preserving the limits of the educational objective in the colleges of Sharia and Law. And I distinguished in this classification the basic maxims from the other subsidiary maxims derived from them, so the basic maxims came to forty and the subsidiaries to fifty-nine. And I arranged the forty basic maxims according to their subjects. And I made the maxims related to guardianship and public administration rights, then those related to proof, at the end of all. And I made the consecutive numbering of the maxims up to the last return to the forty basic ones, and I wrote their numerical rank in words not numbers, and in black letter to distinguish them. As for the subsidiary maxims derived from them, I distributed them under those basic maxims, placing under each of them the maxims derived from it in regular letter, and I denoted the enumeration of those subsidiary maxims by the series of alphabetical letters. And I committed that after mentioning each maxim, I would indicate its article number in the Majalla due to the difference in arrangement, to facilitate reference to it in the Majalla and its commentaries. This, and to facilitate review, we will list at the end of the explanation of these maxims their bare texts arranged according to the alphabetical order of their first words, with an indication next to each of the chapter and paragraph number where the maxim and its explanation occur in this book, and the article number in the Majalla. Chapter Ten: The Comprehensive Legal Maxims in Fiqh. 4/81 - The First Maxim: 'Matters are by their intentions' (Majalla (2)). That is, the actions and transactions of a person, whether verbal or actual, differ in their outcomes and legal rulings that depend on them according to the difference in the person's intention from those actions and transactions. So whoever kills another without legal justification, if he was intentional then his act has a ruling, and if he was mistaken then he has another ruling. And whoever says to another: 'Take these dirhams,' if he intended donation it is a gift, otherwise it is a loan requiring return. And whoever picks up a lost item with the intention of taking it for himself is a usurper, but if he picked it up with the intention of preserving it, announcing it, and returning it to its owner when he appears, he is a trustee; so he is not liable if it is destroyed without his transgression or negligence in its preservation, and so on. What is derived from it of maxims: 5/81 - Consideration in contracts is for intentions and meanings, not for words and structures (Majalla (3)). So bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption) is subject to the rules of many contracts, most prominently the rules of pledge (rahn) because this is the intention of the contracting parties in bay' al-wafa' (see: F 8/46). And a gift if a consideration is stipulated in it, like someone saying to another: 'I give you this thing for such-and-such' or 'on condition that you give me such-and-such,' the contract takes the rulings of sale because it has become in its meaning despite the contracting party using the word gift; so the gifted item is returned due to defect, and also the donee recovers the paid consideration if the gifted item is claimed from his hand, and similarly all other rulings of sale. (1) It is reported in the book (al-Mabsut al-Sarakhsi, vol. 23/22) his saying: '... because consideration in contracts is for meanings not for words.' So this wording of the maxim was established in the Hanafi school before the era of Imam al-Sarakhsi, even he used it as a proof. (2) The meaning of 'claim' has preceded, so look (F 2/37 footnote).
Source pages p. 914

Commentary
This passage is an introduction and the first lesson on the legal maxims of the Majalla. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains his methodology for classifying the maxims. He distinguishes forty basic maxims from fifty-nine subsidiary ones, arranged by topic. He then presents the first major maxim: 'الأمور بمقاصدها' (Matters are by their intentions). This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh. The maxim means that the legal consequence of an action depends on the actor's intention. The author provides examples: killing is intentional or mistaken, giving money can be a gift or a loan based on intent, and picking up a lost item makes one a usurper or a trustee based on intent. Then he presents the subsidiary rule: 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني' (Consideration in contracts is for intentions and meanings, not for words and structures). This sub-maxim applies the first maxim to contracts. Examples include bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption), which is treated like a pledge (rahn) because the parties' real intention is security, not sale. Also, a gift with a stipulation for compensation takes the rulings of a sale, because the meaning is sale. A footnote traces this sub-maxim to al-Sarakhsi's al-Mabsut, showing it was established early in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
المقاصد (al-maqāṣid): The intentions or purposes behind an action or transaction that determine its legal ruling.
العقود (al-ʿuqūd): Legal contracts or agreements between parties, considered by their substance (meanings) not merely their wording.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the subsidiary rule 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني', what is the ruling on a gift (hiba) where the donor stipulates a compensation (e.g., 'I give you this for such-and-such')?
A) It remains a pure gift with no return.
B) It takes the rulings of a sale, including liability for defects and return of consideration if the item is claimed.
C) It is void because the word 'gift' is used but compensation is stipulated.
D) It is considered a loan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the real intention behind an action or contract overrides its external form, making intention the decisive factor in legal rulings.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Scholarly Colophon: Gratitude and Continuity
Colophon: author concludes with thanks and hopes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a classical Hanafi scholarly colophon and explain its function in the tradition of ta'lif and fatwa compilation.

Core Idea
A scholarly work ends with gratitude to Allah and hope for continuation—a model of humility and dedication.

This closing paragraph is not merely a formality; it is a statement of scholarly ethics. The author thanks Allah for enabling the completion of this part and expresses his aspiration to continue the series. In the Hanafi tradition, such colophons reflect the mufti’s awareness that all success is from Allah and that knowledge is a continuous journey. This lesson examines the elements of this closing and their significance in the context of fiqh writing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the colophon
The passage mentions three things: completion of the part, praise to Allah, and hope for future completion. Recognize these as standard elements.
2
Understand the linguistic markers
Phrases like 'هذا آخر ما قصدنا' indicate the author's delimitation of content; 'الحمد لله' expresses gratitude; 'رجائي من الله' signals humility and reliance on Allah.
These markers are common in classical Islamic writings.
3
Appreciate the theological dimension
The phrase 'بِنِعْمَتِهِ تَتِمُّ الصَّالِحَاتُ' attributes completion to Allah's grace, reinforcing tawfiq (divine facilitation).
This is a core belief: all success is from Allah.
4
Recognize the scholarly intent
The hope to continue shows that knowledge is never final; a mufti remains a student.
This relates to the adab al-mufti: continuous learning.
5
Relate to iftaa practice
When compiling fatwas, a mufti should conclude with similar humility, acknowledging that only Allah grants complete understanding.
Modern fatwa collections can adopt this style.

Key Takeaway
The scholarly colophon is a reminder that all academic success is by Allah's grace and that knowledge is a continuous pursuit.

Navigating the Fiqh Index: Structure and Cross-References
Alphabetical index of fiqh topics with page references.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate a fiqh topic in the alphabetical index and interpret its cross-references and page references.

Core Idea
An index is a navigation tool, not a legal text; accurate use requires understanding its structure and cross-references.

This passage is an alphabetical index (fihris) of fiqh topics from a multi-volume Hanafi work. It lists terms, sub-entries, and page references, often with cross-references (انظر). As a mufti or researcher, you will frequently use such indexes to locate discussions efficiently. Understanding the structure—how entries are ordered and how cross-references guide you—is essential for effective study and fatwa preparation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the entry type
Determine whether the line is a main entry (bold or standalone term), a sub-entry (indented), or a cross-reference (انظر). Main entries are the primary topic; sub-entries provide specific aspects; cross-references direct you to a related term.
E.g., 'الإبطال' is a main entry; 'انظر : بطلان' is a cross-reference.
2
Interpret the page reference
Page references like '4/58' indicate volume 4, page 58. Some references include 'ح' (marginal note) or other annotations. Understand the reference system of the book (volume/page).
Volume/page format is typical; some references may have extra letters indicating notes or sections.
3
Follow cross-references
When you see 'انظر' (see), turn to the indicated entry. Cross-references connect related topics or synonyms. For example, 'الإبطال' cross-references 'بطلان' (invalidity). This helps you find the fuller discussion.
Cross-references often consolidate dispersed information under a unified heading.
4
Note sub-entries and scope
Sub-entries under a main topic (e.g., under 'الإرث' inheritance: 'إرث الجنين') specify particular applications. They help you locate nuanced rulings without scanning the entire section.
The index may also have see-also (انظر أيضاً) or see-below (انظر أدناه).
5
Apply to locate a topic
Choose a fiqh term you wish to study (e.g., 'إقرار' confession). Find it in the alphabetical list, then note the page references. Turn to those pages in the volume to read the substantive ruling.
Practice with a real multi-volume Hanafi work (e.g., Al-Mabsut or Bada'i al-Sana'i) reinforces this skill.

Commentary

The text also contains a few corrupted sections due to OCR, such as the garbled phrase after 'الإعارة', which likely represents a cross-reference. This index is typical for multi-volume fiqh works, facilitating navigation. The Hanafi school's terminological nuances are embedded in the indexed terms (e.g., 'أرش' for compensation, 'إقرار' for confession). Note that this index does not present rulings or reasoning; it is purely organizational.

Navigating the Hanafi Fiqh Index: Key Concepts and Maxims
Alphabetical index of subjects and references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use a Hanafi fiqh index as a navigational tool and identify key legal concepts and maxims referenced within.

Core Idea
An index is a map of legal knowledge — it reveals the structure and priorities of the madhhab.

This passage is an alphabetical index from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. While it provides no detailed rulings, it serves as a crucial entry point for navigating the treatise. By understanding the entries and cross-references, you will gain insight into the scope of Hanafi jurisprudential inquiry, including foundational usul concepts like istihsan, istishab, and maxims about dropping of rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the index
Note that the index is alphabetical and provides page references in the format volume/page (e.g., 2/46). This helps locate discussions within the treatise.
2
Locate key usul entries
Find entries such as 'al-istihsan', 'al-istishab', 'al-istislah'. These are foundational concepts in Hanafi legal methodology.
The index also mentions al-Shafi'i's rejection of istihsan.
3
Note the quoted maxims
Several legal maxims appear: 'The original remains as it was', 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by its deprivation', 'If the original falls, the branch falls'.
These maxims are standalone principles used in Hanafi fatwa.
4
Recognize cross-references
Entries like 'See: claim' show how the index links related topics. This helps in comprehensive study.
5
Understand the scope
The index covers personal status, contracts, property, and legal methodology, reflecting the breadth of the treatise.
No substantive rulings are given; the index directs to where they are discussed.

Commentary
This passage is an alphabetical subject index from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. It lists fiqh topics with page references to the main text (volume number/page). Although it provides no substantive legal rulings, it serves as a navigational tool for the treatise. The entries include key Hanafi concepts such as istihsan, istishab, istislah, and various maxims (e.g., 'Whoever hastens something...'). The index reflects the structure of the work, covering personal status, contracts, property, and legal methodology. No madhhab-specific rulings are presented here.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Listed as an entry with page references; in Hanafi usul, istihsan is a method of departing from strict analogy for a stronger reason.
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): Entry with a reference to the maxim 'The principle is to remain as it was' (الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان).
الإسقاط (al-isqat): Entry covering the dropping of rights, with maxims such as 'If the original falls, the branch falls' and 'What has fallen does not return'.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Using the Alphabetical Index: A Navigation Tool
Alphabetical index of topics and page numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the function of a fiqh index and correctly locate a topic using its cross-references without misinterpreting the entries as legal rulings.

Core Idea
An index is a map, not a madhhab text: it tells you where to look, not what to rule.

This passage is an alphabetical index from Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm'. It lists key fiqh topics like capacity (ahliyya), invalidity (butlan), and sale (bay') with page references and cross-references. As a mufti or student, you must learn to use such indices efficiently without confusing brief entry phrases with actual legal content. This lesson trains you to treat the index as a navigation tool and avoid deriving rulings from its condensed notes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the index structure
Notice the alphabetically arranged headings (e.g., أ, ب). Each heading lists sub-entries with page numbers. Some entries include cross-references like 'انظر: ...' (see: ...).
The index is not a continuous legal discussion; it is a reference list.
2
Distinguish between entries and cross-references
Some lines are direct entries (e.g., 'أهلية الوجوب' with page numbers), while others direct you elsewhere (e.g., 'أهلية التصرف : انظر أهلية الأداء'). Understand the arrow symbol and the word 'انظر'.
Cross-references help avoid duplication and guide you to the primary discussion.
3
Interpret abbreviated notes
The index sometimes includes a brief remark, e.g., 'إذا بطل الشيء بطل ما في ضمنه' under 'بطلان'. This is a summary of a rule, not a detailed proof. Always go to the actual page for full context.
These snippets are aides-mémoire, not authoritative legal statements.
4
Apply to locate a topic in the book
In a digital madrasa, use the PDF page numbers directly.

فهرس هجائي (fihris hijā’ī): Alphabetical index listing topics and page numbers for quick navigation.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Using the Fiqh Index: Navigating Topics and Cross-References
Alphabetical index with page references and cross-references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a fiqh index, locate specific topics using volume and page references, and interpret cross-references to trace related discussions in a multi-volume work.

Core Idea
A fiqh index is a map: it organizes topics, links related entries, and directs the researcher to the exact location of rulings.

This lesson introduces you to the alphabetical index from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam'. While often overlooked, such indices are indispensable tools for a mufti or researcher. They allow swift navigation of vast jurisprudential material, reveal how topics interrelate, and provide the first step in scholarly investigation. By learning to parse this index, you gain a practical skill for any multi-volume fiqh reference.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Index Structure
Observe that the index is alphabetical (Arabic abjad order). Each main entry (e.g., 'البيع' – Sale) is followed by sub-entries indented or preceded by a dash. Volume and page numbers are given in the format 'volume/page' (e.g., 4/47).
Some entries have multiple references separated by commas or 'and'. Note the use of 'انظر' (see) for cross-references.
2
Parse a Single Entry
Choose one entry, e.g., 'بيع المضامين' (sale of unborn animals). Note the references: 4/47, 5/14/54. The first number is the volume, the second is the page. Some entries have three numbers (e.g., 5/14/54) – here the second number may indicate part or chapter; check the book's conventions.
Entry 'بيع المعاملة عند متأخري الحنفية' includes a comment 'هو حيلة ربوية' (it is a usurious stratagem) – such brief annotations can be important for fatwa.
3
Follow Cross-References
Locate an 'انظر' (see) entry, e.g., 'الملامسة) المنابذة (انظر: بيع' – meaning 'Touching (throwing) see: Sale'. This directs you to the main entry 'بيع' for related rulings. Trace how sub-entry 'بيع الملامسة' is listed under 'بيع' with its own references.
Cross-references prevent duplication and show connections between topics.
4
Identify Major Thematic Clusters
Scan the index for recurring themes. Notice groups like 'البيع' (sale) with many sub-entries, 'البينة' (evidence), 'التصرف' (disposition). These indicate the book's emphasis. Also note legal maxims (e.g., 'التابع لا يفرد بالحكم') listed under their Arabic first word.
Clusters help you predict where to find related discussions even if not explicitly listed under one heading.
5
Apply the Index to Locate a Topic
Given a research question like 'What is the ruling on a promise to sell?', find the entry 'الوعد بالبيع' (promise to sell) which points to page 4/30. Alternatively, look under 'البيع' sub-entry. Practice finding several topics.
Multiple entry paths exist; the index user chooses the quickest route.

Commentary
This passage is an alphabetical index of fiqh topics and issues from the book 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It lists key terms and their page references, serving as a navigational tool. The index covers various subjects including sales, evidence, causality, dispositions, and legislation. It is typical of front matter in classical fiqh works to help readers locate specific discussions. The references include volume and page numbers from the original work.

Key Terms
انظر (unzur): ‘See’ – a cross-reference directing the reader to a related entry.
بيع المضامين (bay' al-madamīn): Sale of unborn animals, a type of invalid sale due to gharar.
التابع لا يفرد بالحكم (al-tābi' lā yufradu bi al-ḥukm): Legal maxim: the subordinate is not given an independent ruling.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Navigating the Hanafi Fiqh Index
Alphabetical index for locating fiqh topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the alphabetical index to locate Hanafi fiqh topics and interpret cross-references for efficient research.

Core Idea
An index is a map; the mufti must know the terrain.

This passage is an alphabetical index (fihris hijai) from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam. While it does not contain substantive rulings, it is essential for navigating the vast terrain of Hanafi fiqh and usul. A skilled researcher or mufti must be able to use such indexes to quickly locate discussions on specific topics, cross-references, and page numbers. This lesson will equip you with that skill.

Guided Path
1
Identify the entry heading
Each entry begins with a key term (e.g., التصرف). Read the Arabic term and note any subentries or cross-references.
Entries are alphabetically arranged by the first letter.
2
Locate page references
After the term, you may see page numbers (e.g., 12/5-13). The first number is the volume, the second is the page range. This tells you exactly where to find the discussion.
Numbers like 1/72 refer to volume 1, page 72.
3
Interpret cross-references
Some entries have انظر (see) or entries in parentheses. For example, 'التخيير (مقابل اللزوم) انظر: الخيار'. This means the main discussion is under 'الخيار', and 'التخيير' redirects you.
Cross-references help you find related concepts.
4
Note the scope of the term
Some entries have subentries, e.g., التصرف has many subcategories like التصرف الإنشائي. This shows the author's detailed classification.
Understanding the scope helps you quickly assess relevance.

Commentary
This passage is an alphabetical index (fihris hijai) of topics and issues covered in the book 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It serves as a quick reference for readers to locate discussions on various fiqh concepts, such as arbitration (tahkim), specification (takhsis), transactions (tasarruf), custom (urf), and many others. The index provides page numbers and cross-references. It is not a lesson but a navigational tool. The entries reflect the rich scope of the work, covering both classical fiqh theories and contemporary applications.

Key Terms
التحكيم (al-tahkim): Arbitration; listed as a topic in the index.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Specification of a general text; especially mentioned in the context of waqf lands.
التصرف (al-tasarruf): Legal disposition; a major topic with many subcategories in the index.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Navigating the Fiqh Subject Index
Alphabetical index of topics with page references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to navigate the alphabetical subject index of Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm to locate discussions on specific fiqh topics and cross-references.

Core Idea
A subject index unlocks the legal content of a fiqh text — master the index to master the book.

This lesson introduces the alphabetical subject index (فهرس هجائي) from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa. While not containing substantive legal rulings, the index is an essential research tool for any mufti or student of fiqh. Learning to read it efficiently saves time and ensures you access the right discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the entry word
Locate the Arabic term (e.g., الرضا) and note its page references. The entry is bolded or listed alphabetically.
Page references are given as volume/page (e.g., 4/83).
2
Interpret cross-references
When you see 'انظر' (see), follow the linked entry for additional material. For example, 'الرجوع انظر: حق الرجوع' directs you to another entry.
Cross-references broaden your search.
3
Distinguish main entries from subentries
Subentries are indented or listed under a main entry with a bullet or new line. For example, under 'الرضا', there is a subentry 'عيوب الرضا'.
Subentries indicate specific aspects of a topic.
4
Note volume and page format
Understand the numbering system: '4/83' means volume 4, page 83. A fraction like '1/34' means volume 1, page 34. The letter 'ح' after a page number indicates a footnote (حاشية).
This format is common in modern fiqh references.
5
Use the index to build a research path
For a given fatwa question, list relevant index entries, then locate the pages. For example, for a question about bribery, check 'الرشوة' and also 'الرشوة' under related topics like 'الهدية' if cross-referenced.
This method ensures comprehensive coverage.

Commentary
This passage is the alphabetical subject index (فهرس هجائي) from the book 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It lists topics from ra to seen, each followed by page references to the main text. The index serves as a navigation tool for the reader to locate discussions of specific fiqh concepts, rulings, and legal maxims within the book. Entries include cross-references (انظر). This is not a substantive legal discussion but rather a reference guide.

Key Terms
فهرس هجائي (fihris hijā'ī): Alphabetical subject index; a list of topics arranged by Arabic alphabet with page references.
انظر (unẓur): Cross-reference marker meaning 'see'; directs the reader to another entry or section.
ح (ḥā'): Abbreviation for 'حاشية' (footnote); indicates the reference is to a footnote on that page.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the abbreviation 'ح' after a page number indicate in the index?
A) A footnote (حاشية) reference
B) An important ruling
C) A cross-reference to another entry
D) A correction or erratum

Key Takeaway
The alphabetical subject index is a gateway to the fiqh discussions in Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi; master it to navigate the text efficiently.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
Reading the Alphabetical Index in Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam
Alphabetical index of legal terms and page references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate a legal term in the index and identify the corresponding page reference for its full discussion.

Core Idea
An index is a map; learn to read it efficiently to access Hanafi rulings.

This lesson teaches you how to navigate the alphabetical index of legal terms from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam. You will practice parsing entries, interpreting page numbers, and understanding the organization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the headword
Find the Arabic term (e.g., 'السكوت') in bold or as main entry. In the index, each headword starts a new section.
2
Read subentries
Note any subheadings or specific topics listed under the headword (e.g., 'سكوت البكر عند استثمارها في التزويج'). These direct you to particular discussions.
3
Interpret the page reference
Understand the reference format (e.g., '2/32' likely means volume 2, page 32 or section 32). The index uses a consistent system throughout the work.
Some references may combine volume and page numbers.
4
Locate cross-references
Look for 'انظر' (see) references that point to related entries (e.g., 'السكوت ... انظر : الإسقاط'). These help expand your research.

Commentary
This passage is an alphabetical index from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It lists legal themes and maxims with page references. Key entries include the principle that rulings change with time (لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان), and legal maxims on silence, conditions, and blocking means. This index serves as a navigation tool for the book's discourses on Hanafi usul al-fiqh and qawa'id fiqhiyyah.

Key Terms
السكوت (al-sukut): Silence in a context requiring explanation is treated as a clear statement (بيان).
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that must be observed as much as possible, and a condition contrary to Shari'ah is void.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
12 min
The Legal Maxim: Rulings Change with Changing Times
Index entry for a legal maxim on temporal change of rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the adaptability of rulings to time and give one practical example.

Core Idea
Time transforms rulings: circumstances can modify legal judgments within the Shari'ah framework.

This lesson examines the legal maxim 'It is not denied that rulings change with changing times,' indexed in Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam. We'll explore its basis in Hanafi usul and its application in fatwa, understanding that while core texts remain fixed, their application can evolve with temporal needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
Locate the maxim in the index: 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان 46/81'. Understand that it is a recognized legal principle.
The index does not provide the full discussion; you must consult the referenced pages.
2
Understand the maxim's meaning
Rulings derived through ijtihad may change with changing times due to alterations in custom, need, or public interest. However, rulings based on clear textual evidence (nass) remain constant.
The maxim applies primarily to non-textual ('illah-based) rulings.
3
Identify the scope of application
It applies in areas like mu'amalat (transactions), criminal penalties (e.g., ta'zir), and other matters where 'urf (custom) and necessity play a role.
4
Apply to a classic case: price-fixing (tas'ir)
The Prophet (SAW) prohibited price-fixing in general, but later Hanafi scholars allowed it when necessary due to corruption of the times (fasad al-zaman). This is an application of the maxim.
See index references 'جواز تسعير قيم الأشياء عند الاقتضاء 25/81, 7/35'.

This passage is an alphabetical index from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It lists legal themes and maxims with page references. Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
14 min
Silence as an Explanation and Conditions in Hanafi Law
Index entries for silence and conditions maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that silence in a context of need is considered an explanation, and distinguish it from the general rule that no statement is attributed to a silent person.

Core Idea
Silence speaks when explanation is needed; conditions must be observed as much as possible.

This lesson explores two related maxims from the index: (1) Silence in a context requiring explanation is itself an explanation, and (2) Conditions must be observed as much as possible. These principles govern contracts, family law, and legal capacity in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim on silence
The maxim 'السكوت في معرض الحاجة إلى البيان بيان' means that when a person remains silent in a situation where an explanation is expected, that silence is treated as consent or a statement. Conversely, 'لا ينسب إلى ساكت قول' means that generally no statement is attributed to a silent person, but this is overridden when explanation is needed.
The index references these under 'السكوت' with pages 2/32 and 13/81.
2
Identify applications of the silence maxim
A key application is the silence of a virgin when asked about her marriage: her silence is considered consent (see index reference 10/69, 8/71). Also, silence in legal proceedings can indicate approval.
3
Understand the maxim on conditions
The maxim 'يلزم مراعاة الشرط بقدر الإمكان' means conditions must be observed as much as possible. This is balanced by 'كل شرط يخالف أصول الشريعة باطل' - any condition contrary to Shari'ah is void.
Index references under 'الشرط' include 79/81, 20/83, and 22/83.
4
Apply the condition maxim to a contract
In a sale contract, if a condition is valid (e.g., delivery on a specific date), it must be honored. If it contradicts Shari'ah (e.g., interest), it is void but the contract may remain valid.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Navigating the Fihris Hijaa'i: Using the Index as a Mufti's Tool
Index showing how to locate topics alphabetically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate any fiqh or usul topic in the alphabetical index and interpret the volume/page reference system.

Core Idea
An alphabetical index is the mufti's map to the book's treasury of rulings.

This lesson introduces the alphabetical index (fihris hijaa'i) that concludes the book. While it may seem merely organizational, a mufti must be able to quickly navigate such indexes to retrieve rulings on specific topics. The index here covers entries from the letters S and Sh, including key terms like 'silence', 'sultan', 'Sunnah', 'condition', and 'partnership'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the index's title and scope
Read the heading 'فهرس هجائي للمباحث والمسائل' to confirm this is an alphabetical index of topics and issues. Note the letters covered (here S and Sh).
The index is not exhaustive; it covers only topics discussed in the book.
2
Understand the alphabetical ordering
Scan the entries to see the Arabic alphabetical order (e.g., السكوت then السلطان then السنة). Each entry is a topic heading.
Some entries have sub-entries indented or preceded by a dash.
3
Interpret page references
Each entry has page numbers like 8/71. The number before the slash is the volume, and after is the page within that volume. This is a common reference system in multivolume fiqh works.
Some numbers may have parentheses, e.g., (13/81), indicating a more detailed discussion.
4
Use cross-references (انظر)
Many entries have 'انظر: see' directing you to another heading for more information. For example, under 'السلف أو السلم' there is 'انظر: البيع'.
These cross-references link related topics across the index.
5
Practice locating a topic
Choose a topic from the index, e.g., 'الشرط (condition)', and find its page numbers. Identify whether there are sub-categories and cross-references.
This skill is essential for efficient research.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Understanding Al-Shart (Condition) in Hanafi Usul
Index entry for 'condition' with subcategories and maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditions into valid (صحيح), corrupt (مفسد), and void (باطل) according to Hanafi school, and apply the maxim that every condition contradicting Shariah is void.

Core Idea
Every condition that contradicts the principles of Shariah is void; valid conditions are binding in Hanafi fiqh.

The index entry for 'الشرط' (condition) reveals the extensive treatment of this topic in the book. Conditions are a central element in contracts, and Hanafi fiqh has detailed rules distinguishing between valid, corrupt, and stipulative conditions. The index mentions several subcategories and cross-references, indicating the depth of discussion. This lesson extracts the key Hanafi principles from the referenced pages.

Guided Path
1
Read the index entry for 'الشرط'
Examine the index lines starting with 'الشرط' through the end of the section. Note the sub-entries like 'الشرط الصحيح', 'الشرط المفسد', 'الشرط الشرعي', etc.
The index includes page references for each sub-type.
2
Identify the Hanafi maxims on conditions
From the index, you see maxims like 'كل شرط يخالف أصول الشريعة باطل' (Every condition contradicting Shariah foundations is void) and 'يلزم مراعاة الشرط بقدر الإمكان' (Observing the condition as much as possible is necessary). These are key usul rules.
These maxims are referenced at page 20/83 and 79/81 respectively.
3
Classify conditions into valid and corrupt
The index distinguishes between 'الشرط الصحيح' (valid condition) and 'الشرط المفسد' (corrupt condition). A valid condition is one that does not contradict Shariah and is binding. A corrupt condition nullifies the contract if it is a stipulation that violates a principle.
Hanafi school holds that a corrupt condition makes the contract fasid (defective).
4
Understand the role of custom (عرف) in conditions
The index includes references to 'تأثير العرف في الشروط العقدية' (effect of custom on contractual conditions) and 'تأثير العرف في تصحيح الشرط المفسد' (effect of custom in rectifying a corrupt condition). Custom can elevate a corrupt condition to valid if it is customary.
This is a distinctive Hanafi principle: custom is a source of law.
5
Apply the Hanafi position on freedom of stipulation
The index contrasts the Hanbali school's flexibility (see 'مزايا المذهب الحنبلي في حرية الاشتراط') with the Hanafi more restrictive approach. Hanafi limits conditions to those not contradicting the contract's purpose.
This ikhtilaf is important for fatwa when dealing with modern contracts.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
7 min
Al-Shirkah (Partnership) in Hanafi Fiqh: Key Concepts
Index entry for 'partnership' with types and ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the types of partnership (shirkat al-milk, shirkat al-'aqd, mudarabah, etc.) and understand the Hanafi rule that partnership contracts are generally non-binding (ja'iz).

Core Idea
Hanafi partnership contracts are inherently non-binding; partners may withdraw unless they stipulate otherwise.

The index entry for 'الشركة' (partnership) lists several types: shirkat al-milk (co-ownership), shirkat al-'aqd (contractual partnership), mudarabah, musaqah, and muzara'ah. It also notes that partnership contracts are 'غير لازمة' (non-binding). This lesson unpacks these classifications and the Hanafi legal position, crucial for muftis dealing with modern joint ventures and investment accounts.

Guided Path
1
Read the index entry for 'الشركة'
Examine the lines under 'الشركة' which include page references and a note saying 'غير لازمة' (non-binding). Note the sub-entries: شركة المزارعة, شركة المساقاة, شركة المضاربة, شركة الملك.
The entry also references 'أحكام الأساسية لعقد الشركة' (basic rulings of partnership contract).
2
Understand the distinction between shirkat al-milk and shirkat al-'aqd
Shirkat al-milk (co-ownership) is joint ownership of property, not a contract. Shirkat al-'aqd is a contractual partnership where parties agree to share profits and losses. The index references 'شركة الملك' separately from other types.
This distinction is fundamental: co-ownership rules differ from partnership contract rules.
3
Identify the types of contractual partnerships
Mudarabah (profit-sharing), musaqah (irrigation partnership), and muzara'ah (sharecropping) are mentioned. Each has specific rules.
Mudarabah is particularly important for Islamic finance.
4
Note the non-binding nature of partnership contracts
The index explicitly states 'غير لازمة' meaning the contract is not binding (ja'iz). Either partner may withdraw at any time, unless they have stipulated a fixed term.
This is a key Hanafi position; other schools may consider it binding.
5
Apply the ruling: partnership as a non-binding contract
If a partner withdraws, the partnership dissolves. However, if they have invested labor or capital, they must be compensated for their share.
This affects modern joint ventures where partners expect stability.

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Navigating the Fiqh Index: A Gateway to Legal Terminology
Alphabetical index of topics and page references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and purpose of a fiqh index and extract key legal terms and maxims for further study.

Core Idea
An index is a roadmap: each entry leads to a full legal discussion elsewhere.

This passage is an index (fihris) from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. It lists hundreds of fiqh topics alphabetically with page numbers to the main text. While it contains no rulings itself, it is a powerful tool for locating discussions on key concepts like harm (darar), guarantee (daman), and legal capacity. Understanding how to read and use such an index is essential for efficient legal research.

Guided Path
1
Identify the organizational principle
Notice that entries are sorted by Arabic letters (e.g., الصغر, الصفة, الصفقة). The index groups terms by root letters.
This is a standard 'fihris hijā'ī' (alphabetical index).
2
Distinguish main entries from cross-references
Some entries have 'انظر:' (see) directing to another entry (e.g., 'الصغير انظر: أهلية'). Others have sub-entries (e.g., 'الصغير المأذون والمحجور').
Cross-references help you find related discussions.
3
Locate key legal maxims under 'الضرر'
Under 'الضرر' (harm), the index lists well-known qawāʿid like 'الضرر يزال' and 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار'. These are brief but point to full discussions on pages referenced.
These maxims are foundational in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Analyze the entry 'الضمان' (guarantee)
Under 'الضمان', numerous specific issues are listed: guarantee of destruction, guarantee of hired items, etc. Each has a page number for detailed study.
This shows the breadth of the concept in Hanafi law.
5
Practice navigating from index to content
Pick any entry (e.g., 'الخراج بالضمان') and note its page reference (81/81). In a real study session, you would turn to that page to read the full ruling.
This skill is crucial for using classical fiqh texts.

فهرس هجائي للمباحث والمسائل

الصغر
في عوارض الأهلية ٦/٦٥
الصغير

Commentary
This passage is a subject index (fihris) from the book al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It lists key fiqh topics alphabetically (by Arabic letters) along with page references to the main body of the work. The index covers a wide range of legal concepts, including legal capacity (ahliyya), contracts, harm (darar), necessity (darura), taxes, and guarantee (daman). Each entry corresponds to discussions elsewhere in the book. For example, under 'الصغر' (minority) we see references to impediments to legal capacity; under 'الضرر' (harm) we see many well-known legal maxims like 'الضرر يزال' (harm must be eliminated) and 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار' (no harm and no reciprocating harm). This index serves as a navigational tool for readers to locate specific rulings and discussions within the larger work. It is not a legal text in itself but a front-matter component.

Key Terms
فهرس هجائي (fihris hijā'ī): Alphabetical index of topics and issues in the book.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability, guarantee, or compensation for loss or damage.
الصغر (al-ṣighar): Minority, the state of being a minor, affecting legal capacity.
الضرر (al-ḍarar): Harm or injury, a key concept in tort liability and legal maxims.
المباشر (al-mubāshir): Direct cause (of harm).
المتسبب (al-mutasabbib): Indirect cause (of harm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A fiqh index is an essential navigational tool; mastering its use allows efficient access to any legal ruling within the work.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
Navigating the Fihris Hijā'ī: Using the Alphabetical Index in Hanafi Fiqh Research
Alphabetical index of fiqh topics with page references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate fiqh discussions using an alphabetical index and identify the structure of page references in al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Āmm.

Core Idea
An index is your map to the fiqh library — it points to where rulings live, not the rulings themselves.

This lesson examines the alphabetical index (fihris hijā’ī) from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Āmm. While an index contains no substantive legal rules, it is an essential tool for a mufti to quickly locate relevant discussions. Mastering the index format will save you hours in research.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Recognize that entries are Arabic terms with page references. Note the use of 'انظر' (see) to cross-reference.
Some entries have sub-entries indented under a main term.
2
Interpret page references
Understand the format e.g., '10/730kh' likely means volume 10, page 730 (with 'kh' possibly marking a footnote). Also note mixed numbers.
The slash separates volume and page; multiple references are comma-separated.
3
Practice locating topics
Use the index to find a specific topic (e.g., ضمان) and follow the cross-references to locate discussions.
Write down page numbers and check cross-references to related terms.

Commentary
This is an alphabetical index (fihris hijā’ī) from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Āmm by Muṣṭafá Aḥmad al-Zarqā. It lists key fiqh terms – such as ḍamān (damages), ṭalāq (divorce), ʿādah (custom), etc. – with page references to the main text. The index is a navigational tool; it does not contain substantive legal rules. Students should use it to locate discussions of specific topics within the book. The entries sometimes include abbreviated references: e.g., '10/730kh' likely indicates volume/page, though some references are unclear due to OCR corruption.

Key Terms
فهرس هجائي (fihris hijā’ī): An alphabetical index of topics and issues with page numbers.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability or damages, a key concept in Hanafi mu‘āmalāt.
طلاق (ṭalāq): Divorce; its rules and conditions in Hanafi fiqh.
عادة (ʿādah): Custom; a source of law in Hanafi usul when consistent.
ظاهر (ẓāhir): Apparent; used in legal evidence (ẓāhir al-ḥāl).
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): Necessity; legal principle allowing exceptions in dire situations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An alphabetical index is a navigational tool that directs the reader to page references; it does not contain substantive fiqh rulings.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Navigating the Fiqh Index: Key Terms Starting with 'Ayn
Alphabetical index of fiqh topics starting with ع

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate discussions on fiqh topics such as custom ('urf), contract ('aqd), and agnatic heirs ('asabah) using an alphabetical index.

Core Idea
An index is a navigational tool, not a source of rulings; use it to find relevant passages.

This lesson introduces you to the index pages from al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. These pages list fiqh topics beginning with the Arabic letter 'Ayn (ع) with page references. Understanding how to read such an index is crucial for efficient research in classical fiqh texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the head entry
Look for the bolded main terms (e.g., العرف, العقد, العصبة). These are the primary topics listed in alphabetical order.
Head entries are usually the most general concepts.
2
Scan subentries
Under each head entry, there are subentries separated by slashes or line breaks. For example, under العرف you find general, practical, verbal custom.
Subentries specify different aspects or types of the main topic.
3
Note page references
Each subentry has a page number like 4/68 meaning volume 4, page 68. These refer to the main text where the topic is discussed.
Page references allow you to jump directly to the relevant section.
4
Observe cross-references
Terms like 'انظر:' (see) indicate related entries elsewhere in the index. For example, under العقد you see 'انظر: العقد (عقود معينة)' meaning see specific contracts like sale, gift, etc.
Cross-references help you find connected topics.
5
Interpret the structure
The index covers both general concepts (e.g., custom theory) and specific rulings (e.g., tithe on a minor's property). Use it as a map to locate detailed discussions in the main work.
Understanding the hierarchy of entries is key to efficient research.

Commentary
This passage is an index from the book 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It lists fiqh topics beginning with the Arabic letter 'Ayn (ع). The index provides page references to the main text, allowing readers to locate discussions on various subjects such as custom ('urf), contracts ('aqd), and related subcategories. The entries include both general concepts (e.g., general custom, practical custom) and specific rulings (e.g., the tithe on a minor's wealth). The index also shows cross-references (e.g., 'see: will'). As a front matter, this page serves as a navigational tool, not a source of legal rulings. The teacher should note that this index reflects the structure of the book and helps students locate relevant discussions for their studies.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom or common practice; a source of law in the Hanafi school when not contradicting primary sources.
العقد (al-'aqd): Contract; the agreement between two parties that creates legal obligations.
العصبة (al-'asabah): Agnatic heirs who inherit after the Qur'anic heirs have taken their shares.
العشر (al-'ushr): The tithe; a tax or duty of one-tenth on agricultural produce under certain conditions.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Navigating the Fiqh Index: Key Classifications of Contracts
Alphabetical index of fiqh topics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate fiqh topics using an alphabetical index and identify the key classifications of contracts in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Indexes are scholarly tools; knowing contract classifications unlocks the structure of Hanafi transactional law.

This lesson introduces you to the internal roadmap of a major fiqh reference work. The alphabetically arranged index lists hundreds of topics, with a strong emphasis on contract theory (العقود). By understanding the categories listed—valid, voidable, commutative, etc.—you will gain a bird's-eye view of how Hanafi jurists organize transactional law. This is not a lesson on rulings, but on how to find them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the index
Notice that the index is in Arabic alphabetical order. Each entry has a bold headword (e.g., العقد) followed by subentries indented below, often with cross-references (انظر:).
Arabic alphabetical order follows the Abjad sequence; here it is standard Hijai.
2
Recognize major contract categories
Look for key adjectives: فاسدة (voidable), صحيحة (valid), فورية (instantaneous), مستمرة (continuous), أصلية (original), تبعية (subsidiary). These are the axes along which contracts are classified in Hanafi fiqh.
Each classification affects legal consequences.
3
Note cross-references to other topics
The index uses cross-references (e.g., العقد الفوري والمستمر) and redirects (see:). This helps you navigate between related concepts.
Cross-references are indicated by 'انظر:' or simply 'انظر:'.
4
Locate a specific topic using page numbers
Each entry ends with a page reference (e.g., ٢/٣٨). The first number is the volume, the second is the page. Use these to find the detailed discussion.
Volume numbers are often in Arabic numerals (٢) and page numbers in Western digits (38).
5
Recognize legal maxims as index entries
Some entries are legal maxims, e.g., 'الحكم يدور مع علته' and 'العبرة للغالب الشائع لا للنادر'. These maxims are key to legal reasoning and appear as topics.
These maxims are part of the Hanafi usul al-fiqh and are cited in fatwas.

Commentary
This passage is an index. It lists fiqh topics alphabetically in Arabic, with page references to the book's contents. The index covers a wide range of subjects related to contracts (عقود), including classifications such as original vs. subsidiary, valid vs. voidable, commutative vs. donation-based, etc. It also includes entries on penalties (عقوبات), cause (علة), knowledge of the judge, defects in consent, and general legal maxims such as 'The ruling follows its cause' (الحكم يدور مع علته) and 'Consideration is for the prevalent and common, not the rare' (العبرة للغالب الشائع لا للنادر). This index serves as a quick reference for readers to locate discussions on specific fiqh topics within the book.

Key Terms
العقد (al-‘aqd): The contract; a legally binding agreement between two parties.
العقود الفاسدة (al-‘uqūd al-fāsidah): Voidable contracts; contracts that are defective in some essential element but may be validated.
الحكم يدور مع علته (al-ḥukm yadūru ma‘a ‘illatih): A legal maxim: The ruling revolves with its cause (ratio legis); whenever the cause exists, the ruling applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Navigating the Alphabetical Index of Fiqh Topics
Alphabetical index of fiqh topics with references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use an alphabetical index from a Hanafi fiqh work to locate specific topics, identify key terms, and follow cross-references.

Core Idea
An index is a map of knowledge: it reveals the structure, scope, and key concepts of a legal work.

This passage is an alphabetical index from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhī al-ʿĀmm' (General Introduction to Fiqh) by Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā. It lists fiqh terms from the letter Qāf (ق) to Lām (ل), each with volume and page references to the main text. This index is a powerful research tool—it helps you quickly locate discussions on specific topics, identify key legal concepts, and understand the scope of the work. In this lesson, you will learn how to navigate such an index effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the letter range and scan headings
Look at the alphabetical order: the index covers letters Qāf (ق), Kāf (ك), and Lām (ل). Note the main entries (e.g., القياس, الكفالة) and sub-entries indented below them. This structure shows hierarchical relationships.
The Arabic letters are arranged according to the Arabic alphabet.
2
Locate a key term and examine its sub-entries
Find a term like الكفالة (suretyship). Notice it has sub-entries such as 'الكفالة بالدرك' and 'الكفالة بالنفس'. These point to specific topics within the broader subject. Read the associated volume/page numbers (e.g., 5/46, 16/47).
Sub-entries often cover subtypes or related topics.
3
Follow cross-references (انظر) to related terms
Some entries contain 'انظر' (see) directing you to other index entries. For example, under القصاص you see 'انظر : القصاص'—this helps you connect related concepts. Use these to expand your research.
Cross-references prevent duplication and link interconnected topics.

Commentary
This is an alphabetical index (فهرس هجائي) of topics and issues discussed in the book 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. The index covers letters from Qaf (القاف) to Lam (اللام) on these two pages. It lists key fiqh terms such as القراض (mudarabah), القرينة (presumption/indication), القياس (analogy), الكفالة (suretyship), الكلام (speech), اللزوم (bindingness), and many others. Each entry provides volume and page references where the topic is discussed in the main text. Some entries include sub-entries (e.g., under القرينة: العرفية والعقلية, القانونية والقضائية) and cross-references (انظر). This index is a valuable tool for navigating the author's comprehensive exposition of Islamic legal theory, legal maxims, and specific fiqh issues. It reflects the systematic organization of the book, which covers both usul al-fiqh and substantive fiqh from a Hanafi perspective.

Key Terms
القرينة (al-qarīnah): A presumption or indication that helps interpret legal texts or contracts
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning as a source of Islamic law
الكفالة (al-kafālah): Suretyship, a contract guaranteeing the fulfillment of an obligation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Navigating the Alphabetical Fiqh Index
Alphabetical index of fiqh topics with page references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse an alphabetical fiqh index to locate specific topics and interpret the volume/page notation.

Core Idea
An index is a map, not a ruling—it shows where the legal discussion lives.

This lesson introduces the alphabetical index of a comprehensive fiqh encyclopedia. You will learn how to interpret the entries, understand the volume/page notation, and use cross-references to navigate the book efficiently. This skill is essential for any researcher or mufti who needs to quickly locate detailed rulings on specific topics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the entry heading
Find the main term (e.g., الوصية) and note its category if indicated.
Entries are alphabetically sorted.
2
Read the page reference
Numbers are given in the format volume/page. For example, 27/46 means volume 27, page 46.
Arabic numerals may be mixed (e.g., ٠٧/٦٢ = 7/62).
3
Cross-reference (انظر)
If the entry says 'انظر: البيع', it directs you to another main entry for more detail.
Cross-references connect related topics.
4
Subentries and details
Some topics have subentries with specific page references, separated by commas or listed on new lines.
Subentries specify particular aspects, e.g., 'وصية الصغير المميز'.

Commentary
This passage is the alphabetical index of the book, listing key fiqh topics and their page references. It serves as a guide to locate discussions on specific issues such as wills (الوصية), endowments (الوقف), agency (الوكالة), guardianship (الولاية), oaths (اليمين), and certainty (اليقين). The references typically denote volume and page numbers (e.g., 27/46 means volume 27, page 46). This index reflects the comprehensive scope of the work, covering civil transactions, family law, and legal maxims. It also includes cross-references to related entries. The index itself is not a legal ruling but a tool for navigating the book's content.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A will or bequest, a contract that takes effect after death.
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment, permanently dedicating property for charitable or family benefit.
الوكالة (al-wikāla): Agency, a contract where one person authorizes another to act on their behalf.
الولاية (al-wilāya): Guardianship or authority, especially over minors and their property.
اليقين (al-yaqīn): Certainty, a key legal maxim: certainty is not overridden by doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An alphabetical fiqh index is a navigation tool that uses volume/page references and cross-references to guide the reader to detailed discussions.

Introduction and Front Matter

Using Indices in Hanafi Fiqh Works: Navigating Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm
Two indices from a Hanafi fiqh introduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate specific topics and personalities in a Hanafi fiqh work using its subject and biographical indices.

Core Idea
Indices are essential tools for efficient study of comprehensive Hanafi legal texts.

This lesson introduces you to the indices found in Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. The subject index and biographical index are critical for quickly locating discussions on key topics and the positions of major Hanafi scholars. Understanding how to use these indices will greatly enhance your research efficiency in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of index needed
Determine whether you need a subject (topic) or biographical (person) reference. The subject index lists fiqh concepts and rules; the biographical index lists scholars and historical figures.
The maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' appears in the subject index.
2
Locate the entry alphabetically
Both indices are arranged alphabetically by the first letter of the term or name. Scan the relevant section to find your entry.
For example, 'الولاية' under 'و'.
3
Note the page reference(s)
Each entry includes one or more page numbers (e.g., 6/81). These refer to the page and sometimes the paragraph within the book.
Multiple page numbers indicate multiple occurrences.
4
Turn to the referenced page and read the discussion
Locate the indicated page in the main text and study the relevant discussion. The index helps you jump directly to the material.
The biographical index may include brief notes like 'his fatwa on...'.
